NORTHEAST BRAZIL
PIAUI RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Brazil continues to devote considerable attention
to rural development in Northeast Brazil. The POLONORDESTE program of
multi-sectoral development, initiated in 1974, now finances activities in
some 46 priority areas throughout the Northeast. The Bank has partici-
pated in the financing of seven POLONORDESTE rural development projects in
six different states. Experiences from these projects have shown that it
is possible to improve incomes and living standards of the rural poor by
increasing access to appropriate production technologies and supporting
services such as credit and extension. Nonetheless, major constraints on
extending the benefits of the POLONORDESTE program to the poorest segments of
the rural population have been insecurity of land tenure and lack of access
to land. These constraints have severely limited the majority of small
farmers' response to, and receipt of benefits from, improvements in produc-
tion technology and the delivery of services.
1.02      The proposed project, while building on previous integrated rural
development strategies and experiences in Northeast Brazil, would constitute
the first comprehensive attempt to address the land constraints facing
small farmers. A major contribution of the project would be the development,
application, and refinement of replicable mechanisms and procedures to
increase tenure security and access to land in an area where nearly eighty
percent of all farmers are landless. Because of the pioneering nature of the
project, detailed annual planning and continuous monitoring are central
features. These aspects of the project could have a substantial impact on
future rural development efforts throughout Northeast Brazil.
II. LAND IN NORTHEAST BRAZIL
A. Background
2.01      The nine states of Northeast Brazil have a population of about
35 million (1980), about one half of whom live in rural areas. The population
has been growing at a net rate of about 2.2% per year between 1970 and 1980.
Much of the region has a semi-arid climate with periodic serious droughts,
and there are extensive areas of poor soils. Nonetheless, the Northeast
is a significant agricultural region, with about 45% of Brazil's agriculturally
employed population and a major share of the national production of cocoa,
cotton, manioc, beans, and sugar. In 1980, about 30% of the area harvested
in Brazil was in the Northeast. Although some crops such as sugarcane are
grown predominantly on large farms, basic food crops are produced mainly
by small farmers, employing rudimentary technology and obtaining low yields.



Small farmer agricultural development has been hampered by limited access
to working capital and investment credit, rural extension services,
improved seeds and other inputs; market isolation and weak linkages within
the regional economy; and, in some areas, a relatively unfavorable physical
environment. Insecurity of land tenure and lack of access to land have
compounded these difficulties and, in many areas, constitute the basic
constraint on increasing production and productivity among small farmers.
B. Land Tenure Issues
2.02      The pattern of land ownership is highly uneven in Northeast Brazil.
According to the 1975 Census, farms larger than 500 ha (1% of farm establish-
ments) account for more than 40% of all farm area. Some 70% of farms are
under 10 ha and occupy less than 6% of the total farm area. Of the total rural
labor force, only 28% represent landowners and their working family members.
While a small portion of the labor force is comprised of permanent wage
earners on large, modern estates, the vast majority are sharecroppers,
tenants, or illegal occupants on both public and private lands.
2.03      Among sharecroppers and tenants, serious problems exist.  In some
Northeast areas, livestock and sugarcane expansion has severely limited the
quantity of land available for small farmer use.  Rental and sharecropping
arrangements are generally verbal and limited to one crop cycle, contrary to
provisions of Brazilian land law (Estatuto da Terra). Landlords in most areas
require tenants to clear and crop a different area each year, thus receiving a
free labor input for pasture establishment. Land clearing and fencing, in
order to protect crops from free-roaming cattle, occupy a significant portion
of family labor time, restricting opportunities for alternative employment
during the non-agricultural season. Rental fees, while varying from area to
area, are generally 25 to 50% of total annual production. Frequently, they
are paid in kind, and landlords may require full or partial payment before
harvest, when prices are at the highest annual level. Obligatory unpaid
service (sujeicao) to landowners still persists on many of the larger,
traditional estates. Large landowners in many areas control the marketing
and informal credit channels as well. Tenants and sharecroppers are eligible
for official subsidized seasonal credit only after obtaining express written
consent from landowners.
2.04      Small owners in the Northeast are also confronted by a variety
of problems. In most states, land ownership records are incomplete, and
a significant proportion of small owners do not possess proper legal titles
and are thus barred from the official credit system. Frequently, properties
are not of a sufficient size to guarantee family subsistence. As is the
case for sharecroppers and tenants, small owners have virtually no
opportunity to purchase additional lands. This is due not only to the
limited availability of land purchase credit, but also because large owners
are reluctant to sell off their properties in small lots. Land concentration
has increased markedly during the past twenty years in the Northeast.



2.0)5     Large estates in the Northeast demonstrate lower levels of produc-
tivity and land and labor use than do small farms. The smallest farms
employ about 50% of total area in crops, while only 10% is cultivated on the
largest farms. Small farms throughout the Northeast absorb as much as 45
times more labor per hectare than large farms. Farms exceeding 500 ha control
42% of total agricultural area, but contribute only 31% of crop production
and 34% of livestock production. Farms under 10 ha control about 6% of
the land, while contributing 8% to total crop production and 7% to total
livestock production.
C. Land Policies and Institutions
2.06      Although land tenure problems and the comparative inefficiency
of large farms have been acknowledged for many years, Government policy
to date has had a negligible impact on the Northeast land tenure situation.
Limited enforcement of the Brazilian land law (Estatuto da Terra) and the
lack of a specialized judicial system for land matters have impeded progress
toward improving land tenure security for small farmers. Neither large-
scale colonization schemes nor the creation and funding of a number of
rural poverty programs, such as POLONORDESTE, have succeeded in reaching
a substantial number of landless farmers. Recently, however, this situation
has begun to evolve. Limited land-related activities have been included in a
number of POLONORDESTE rural development projects (para 2.07), and systematic
consideration of land tenure issues and possible solutions were incorporated
into the 1981 planning exercises for all 46 POLONORDESTE projects. While
these pilot efforts have experienced implementation delays because of limited
technical and institutional capabilities, they have succeeded in providing
individual states with valuable land-related experience. Land institutes have
now been created in several Northeast states, including Bahia, Ceara, Maranhao,
and most recently, Piaui. In addition, INCRA (National Institute of Colonization
and Agrarian Reform), the federal agency most directly involved in land
matters, has begun to play a more active role in the Northeast. In 1980, a
progressive federal land taxation law was approved. Under the law, tax rates
increase with property size and degree of non-utilization or inefficient
exploitation. As a first step toward universal enforcement of the law, INCRA
has executed land discrimination campaigns in several areas of the Northeast.l/
A regional land discrimination and titling project is currently under
preparation by INCRA. INCRA has also declared parts of the Northeast as
.,priority areas for agrarian reform," and several huge, underutilized estates
have been identified in each state for possible expropriation. Nonetheless,
expropriation is still exceptional, requiring presidential approval, and
Government land policy appears to be focused on land discrimination, purchasing,
and redistribution programs, such as those proposed under the project.
1/ The detailed determination of current land claims, and the identification
and demarcation of lands not legally claimed by private parties which
could be subsequently redistributed.



-4-
D. Bank Involvement In Land Activities
2.07      Some land activities have been included in a number of Bank-
assisted rural development projects in Northeast Brazil. The Bahia Rural
Development Project (Paraguacu) includes a significant land titling component
(some 2,300 titles already granted and some 3,700 in process with a target of
15,000) and a small land purchase component to be executed by a newly created
state land institute. The first Ceara Rural Development Project (Serra da
Ibiapaba) provided for a small land purchase scheme which has thus far benefited
a small number of sharecroppers. The Rio Grande do Norte, Paraiba and Sergipe
Rural Development Projects all have modest land purchase and/or titling
activities currently under implementation. The recently approved second Ceara
Rural Development Project provides support for land discrimination, titling,
and a new state land institute. Additional rural development projects which
would include significant land activities (Maranhao, Rio Grande do Norte II)
are under consideration for possible future Bank financing.
III.  THE STATE OF PIAUI AND THE PROJECT AREA
A. The State
Location and Natural Features
3.01      Piaui has a total area of 250,934 km  and is the third largest state
in Northeast Brazil. It is bordered on the north by the Atlantic Ocean, on the
south by the states of Goias and Bahia, on the west by the states of Maranhao
and Bahia, and on the east by Ceara and Pernambuco. Much of the state (about
83%) is characterized by low-lying, semi-arid plains and tablelands and is
highly sensitive to drought. The state's major waterways are comprised by the
1,485 km Parnaiba River and its numerous tributaries. Average annual rainfall
is highly variable and ranges from 700 to 1,600 mm in the Parnaiba River
valley and from 500 to 1,000 mm in the central and western areas of the state.
Average annual temperature varies between 2OoC and 28oC.
Social and Economic Conditions
3.02      Piaui has a total population of about 2.1 million (1980), of which
about 60% is estimated to be rural. Average population density is 8/km2, the
lowest of any Northeastern state. Teresina, located within the proposed
project area, is the state capital and largest city with a population of about
300,000. Agriculture is the predominant economic activity, employing an
estimated 70% of the economically active population and contributing over 40%
of the total state GDP (1977). Average per capita income for the state as a
whole is about US$300. Income distribution is highly skewed, with the bottom
forty percent receiving about seven percent of total income, while the top



- 5 -
five percent accounts for more than sixty percent of the total. Similar
to other areas of Northeast Brazil, malnutrition and illiteracy rates are
high, particularly in the rural areas.
Agricultural Production and Land Tenure
3.03      Although agriculture continues to be the state's most important
economic sector, its relative importance has been declining in recent years.
Principal products include livestock, babacu nuts, carnauba wax, beans, rice,
cotton, corn, and manioc. With the exception of manioc, total production and
average yields for the state's main crops dropped significantly from 1977 to
1979. While recurring drought conditions are a major contributing factor,
poor soil and water management, coupled with low technology levels, continue
to hamper the state's agricultural development. The most important constraint
to increasing agricultural production and productivity, particularly among
small farmers, has been the state's land tenure situation. According to the
INCRA cadaster, some 88% of the state's farmers, mainly tenants and sharecroppers,
own no land and have no tenure security. Some 72% (151,000) of farms are
under 10 ha and account for only 3% of the state's privately held lands. At
the same time, less than 1% (1,350) of farms exceed 1,000 ha and occupy 42% of
the state's total agricultural area.
Rural Development
3.04      The POLONORDESTE program represents the largest single source
of federal funds for the state, and POLONORDESTE funds account for over 60%
of all public resources going to rural areas. There are currently six ongoing
POLONORDESTE projects in Piaui, including the Delta do Parnaiba project
which would be absorbed by the proposed project. Partially as a result of the
limited impact of these ongoing activities, state government policy has
recognized that improvements in land tenure security and small farmer access
to land, coupled with the development and dissemination of improved technical
packages, are prerequisites for increasing rural incomes and agricultural
production. The proposed project would constitute the first comprehensive
attempt to address land-related issues as part of the state's overall rural
deveLopment effort.
B. The Project Area
Location
3.05      The proposed project area, located in the western portion of the
state along the middle and lower Parnaiba River, has a total area of some
24,300 km2 or about 10% of total state area. While the project area cur-
rent:Ly encompasses 26 municipalities, its limits should be regarded as
provisional. Land acquisition prospects may warrant expansion or the sub-
stitution of some municipalities by adjacent municipalities. Three distinct
subareas, with varying ecological conditions, can be identified within the
project area. The Delta do Parnaiba, currently with ten project municipali-
ties covering 44% of the area, is located in the north and contains all of



- 6 -
Piaui's 66 km coastline. The Teresina subarea, with six project municipali-
ties covering 31% of the area, is centrally located and includes the state's
capital and largest city. The Medio Parnaiba, containing the ten southernmost
project municipalities, occupies some 257 of the area.
Matural Features and Climate
3.06      The project area forms part of the Parnaiba sedimentary basin,
characterized by low-lying tablelands (chapadas) periodically cut by the broad
valleys of the Parnaiba river system. Chapada soils are generally shallow,
sandy latosols, with low to medium productivity, while soils on the slopes
are, for the most part, stony and unsuitable for agriculture. Valley soils,
usually sandy loams with a relatively higher clay content, are the best soils
in the project area. Potential yields of up to two tons of rice per hectare
can be reached in these soils, while about 1.5 tons per hectare is the upper
limit for the chapada soils. Pespite these differences, project area soils are
commonly affected by a low natural chemical fertility and a low water
permeability and moisture retention capacity. Erosion is an increasingly
serious problem. While surface and underground water resources are fairly
abundant, surface water flows are subject to wide seasonal fluctuations, and
groundwater accessibility, in terms of depth and yields, is variable. Average
temperatures range from 26oC to 3OoC with average relative humidity varying
from 40% to 70%. Although total annual rainfall averages more than 1,100 mm,
it is highly concentrated during the rainy season (December to June), with a
three-month peak period from January to March and some variation in its
distribution. Because of this variation, combined with the low moisture
retention capacity of the soils, agricultural production in the project area
is periodically affected by severe drought conditions (Annex 2).
Socio-Economic Conditions
3.07      The project area includes the state's two largest cities, Tere-
sina and Parnaiba. Project area population is about 770,000 (1975), of
which just under 50% is rural. Average rural population density is about
14/km2. Urban population is growing at a much faster rate than rural popu-
lation, largely as a result of increased rural-urban migration. Average
family size is about five persons, similar to that found in other rural
areas in Northeast Brazil.
3.08      Agriculture is the chief economic activity of the area, although
fishing is an important activity along the coast. Average annual rural
per capita income, subject to wide interyearly fluctuations, is about US$200,
well below the relative rural poverty level in Brazil (US$330). Illiteracy,
high infant mortality rates, intestinal diseases, and malnutrition are
widespread.
Land Tenure and Land ITse
3.09      Land ownership in the project area is extremely concentrated.  Of
the 57,700 farmers in the project area, about 22%7 are ownxers. Some 707 ot
the owners, many of whom lack proper titles, are small farmers with holdings
of less than 100 ha. This group controls only 18% of all agricultural lands,



- 7 -
while owners with more than 100 ha control 82% of the land. While the exact
situation is not known, it appears that a considerable amount of public land
has been incorporated illegally into the estates of large holders.
3.10      Landless farmers, estimated at 78% of all project area farmers, are
mainly tenants cultivating small plots (3 ha average) on the larger estates.
Rental contracts, contrary to provisions in federal land laws, are usually
verbal, one-year arrangements, and tenants seldom cultivate the same piece of
land for more than one year. Landlords usually offer for rent lands in need
of clearing, which will be used for cattle-raising after the cropping season.
Rental fees are excessively high in relation to total production (25-50%) and
the average price of land (100-200%). Tenants are not compensated for the
labor they provide for land clearing. Rental fee payment, generally in kind,
may be required before the harvest.
3.11      While the project area possesses considerable agricultural poten-
tial, the land tenure situation has adversely affected agricultural produc-
tion. Despite the relatively low cost of land (average of US$35/ha) compared
to other areas of the Northeast, small owners and tenants have little
opportunity to acquire land because of the limited availability of land
purchase credit and the reluctance of large landowners to sell off small plots.
Tenants, lacking secure access to land, are unwilling and unable to adopt new
technologies, even those which require relatively small amounts of investment.
Small owners, lacking an adequate amount of land for an appropriate fallow
rotation system, are forced to over-utilize their land, resulting in the
progressive deterioration of lands under cultivation.
3.12      At the same time, huge tracts of cultivable land remain idle.
While an estimated 70% (1.7 million ha) of the total project area is suitable
for agriculture, only about 20% to 30% of this land is under cultivation, with
10% (180,000 ha) annually cropped. Land use varies in direct relation to farm
size. Small owners with less than 10 ha usually crop about 80% of their
property; this proportion regularly decreases as farm size increases. Less
than 14% of total area is generally cultivated on properties over 100 ha, and
there are several large properties in the project area where cropped land
accounts for under 5% of total cultivable area.
Present Agricultural Technology and Production
3.13      Low levels of technology and agricultural practices inadequately
adapted to agroclimatic conditions are characteristic of the project area.
Common features include: (a) utilization of the slash and burn system,
with long fallow periods (up to eight years); (b) low labor productivity,
largely the result of high labor coefficients for clearing and fencing and
all-manual cultivation practices; (c) planting of local seed varieties,
with no application of organic or chemical fertilizers or pesticides; and
(d) traditional cropping practices (e.g. use of medium- and long-cycle
varieties; late planting; non-contour cropping) which are not based on sound
water and soil management techniques. There are, however, a few technic-
ally advanced small farmers who have adopted improved techniques, mainly
through the use of animal traction and other forms of mechanization which
improve labor productivity.



- 8 -
3.14      Of the 180,000 ha annually cropped, most of the area is covered
by various forms of intercropping (about 88%) with upland rice, the pre-
dominant crop, accompanied by corn, beans and manioc. Single croppings,
mostly of rice, account for about 12% of the cultivated area. While landless
farmers generally do not have cattle, small husbandry activities (pigs and
poultry) are fairly common. In addition, small owners normally keep small
(three or four head) cattle herds.
3.15      Average yields for the predominant crops under the various forms
of intercropping are very low: about .7 tons/ha for rice; .3 tons/ba for
corn; .2 tons/ha for beans and 4.0 tons/ha for manioc. In addition, present
cropping practices combined with interyearly climatic swings, result in
highly variable yields. In 1979, when severe drought conditions prevailed,
total estimated rice production in the project area was 64,500 tons, only
387 of the estimated quantity which would have been produced under normal
circumstances.
Agricultural Services
3.16      In the past, agricultural services in the project area have been
oriented mainly toward medium and large farmers. This situation has partially
changed in recent years due to the implementation of POLONORDESTE and INAN
(National Food and Nutrition Institute) projects which have a specific small
farmer focus 1/. Agricultural research in Piaui is the responsibility of
EMBPAPA, the Federal Agricultural Research Enterprise, which maintains a
central experimental station in Teresina. Currently, 18 EMATER-PI (the State
Rural Technical Assistance and Fxtension Company) local offices, with a total
of 49 agricultural extension agents and 17 social extensionists, serve the
project area. Agricultural credit is channeled principally through the
agencies of the Government-owned banks. Thirteen project municipalities have
local bank branches. In addition to private agricultural input retailers
located in the larger project municipalities, CIDAPI (the State Agricultural
Development Company) maintains five input supply posts in the project area.
There are eight additional posts operated by the six existing agricultural
cooperatives. The state has recently established a new seed production and
distribution network which should be operational within the next 12 months.
SIMA (the National Agricultural Market Information System) collects project
area price information through its Teresina and Parnaiba agencies. Public
storage facilities, with a total static capacity of 32,000 tons, are located
in six project municipalities. Despite the existence of these services, to
date most institutions have not succeeded in providing effective assistance to
small farmers because of budgetary limitations, the insecure tenure arrange-
ments of most small farmers, and the continued orientation of some institutions
towards the needs of larger farmers.
1/ The activities of the current Delta do Parnaiba POLONORDFSTE project
located within the project area would be absorbed by the proposed
project, with some modifications to ensure overall project consistency.



-9-
Fisheries
3.17      Some 2,500 fishermen exploit the marine resources off Piaui's
66 km coastline. Approximately one half of these fishermen are engaged in
.Eishing as a full-time occupation. Fishermen organizations include various
l:ypes of informal groups and one cooperative. At present, the cooperative has
105 members and limited financial resources.
3.18      In 1978, the state produced about 4,250 tons of fish and shell-
fish. Principal species include mangrove crabs, red snapper, mackerel,
sea bass, and tarpon. Average catch per fisherman (843 kg/year) is
very low and, consequently, annual average income from fishing activities
(US$100 per capita) is very low. These low levels are largely due to the
use of rudimentary technology and inadequate equipment. Although extension
and technical assistance have been offered by EMATER-PI since 1976, very
few fishermen have been reached by this service. Both official banks and
non-institutional sources of credit exist. Non-institutional sources are
tied to marketing which is carried out by private middlemen.
3.19      Auxiliary services attached to the fishing industry are important in
the coastal areas. There are eight boatbuilders, employing mostly hand tools.
Retail outlets in Parnaiba supply engines and mechanical assistance. Ice-
production capacity is insufficient, although a small ice factory to be
operated by the cooperative is presently being constructed with POLONORDESTE
resources. Fish processing and artisan crafts carried out at the household
and village levels represent important additional sources of income and
employment for area residents.
Physical and Social Infrastructure
3.20      Water Development.  Little utilization has been made of the project
area's relatively abundant water resources for agricultural development.
Irrigation is presently limited to a small number of farmers who have
installed their own small irrigation schemes and a DNOCS (National Department
of Works Against the Drought) pilot project of 500 ha benefiting 200 farmers.
Pre-feasibility studies for another 16,000 ha along the Parnaiba River are
currently under way. Although part of the project area would be suitable for
further irrigation development, the state currently does not possess the
institutional capacity to assist in that development.
3.21      The present situation is little better with respect to water
for human and animal consumption. Although in 25 of the 26 proiect
municipalities the urban population is served by adequate water supply
facilities constructed and operated by AGEPISA (The Piaui Water and Sewer
Company), there are almost no adequate water supply systems functioning
in the rural areas. Some wells, reservoirs, and man-made ponds exist,
but most of them lie idle due to lack of maintenance and repair services.
Although rural residents generally have year-round access to water, deficient
water quality is a serious problem. At present, there is no one institu-
tion responsible for rural water supply in the project area, although



- 10 -
CIDAPI (well-drilling), the State Secretariat of Health (wells and sani-
tation), EMATER-PI (technical assistance and training in proper water use)
and FSESP (Special Service Foundation for Health--construction and opera-
tion of water supply systems) have done some work in rural areas elsewhere
in the state.
3.22      Roads.  The federal and state road system is reasonably well
developed within the project area. Feeding into this system is a network
of some 6,540 km of municipal roads (15% of the state's total). Preliminary
information indicates that existing traffic on these roads is at a fairly
low level and seasonally concentrated. Municipal roads, the majority
unpaved, are constructed by private contractors under the supervision and
design specifications of CODERPI (The Piaui Road Development Company).
Primary responsibility for municipal road improvement and maintenance rests
with the municipalities. Due to technical and financial constraints, many of
the roads are poorly maintained.
3.23      Social Services.  Education and health facilities are generally
deficient in the project-s rural areas, although recent steps could bring
significant improvements. Fifteen of the project municipalities are included
in the Bank-financed Northeast Basic Education Project. Health activities,
about one-half financed by federal funds, are carried out by a variety of
agencies under the planning and coordination of the State Secretariat of
Health (SESMA). In the past, SESMA has not provided effective administration
for the rural health sector. In late 1979 a State Coordinating Health Committee
was created to review state health activities, and there are some tentative
plans for the reorganization of SESMA at the state and regional levels.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
Origin
4.01      The state first requested Bank assistance in preparing and imple-
menting a rural development project in October 1977. During the initial
stages of project preparation, it became evident that a major constraint to
improving rural incomes was the lack of secure access to land faced by the
majority of small farmers. Previous Bank experience in other parts of the
Northeast had demonstrated that this constraint could severely limit the
response to, and the benefits derived from, improvements in technology and
the delivery of agricultural services. Consequently, measures to improve
land tenure security and access to land gradually evolved into the principal
focus of the project. The project, in its current form, was prepared during
late 1979 and 1980 by the State Planning Commission (CEPRO) linked to the
Secretariat of Planning, in close cooperation with the Bank and the
executing agencies.



- 11 -
Objectives
4.02      The proposed project would represent the initial phase of a long-
term development strategy and investment program elaborated in accordance with
a specific set of development objectives for the project area. These objectives
include: (a) increasing income and employment opportunities for the rural
poor; (b) improving socio-economic conditions, thereby diminishing the rate
of rural-urban migration; and (c) ensuring an adequate food supply to urban
populations. A significant factor in meeting these objectives would be the
emergence of a strong sector of small-scale agricultural entrepreneurs
specialized in labor-intensive food production. This sector s progressive
utilization of modern technology, including irrigation where possible, would
contribute toward the development of the area's agricultural potential.
In a similar fashion, small fishermen, utilizing improved technology, would
take advantage of the coastal area's considerable marine resources, consequently
increasing fish production and fishing incomes.
4.03       Within the general framework for the POLONORDESTE rural develop-
ment program, the specific objectives of the proposed project would be:
(a) to improve the standard of living and socio-economic conditions, including
certain nutritional aspects, of a target group of low-income, small farmers
and fishermen; and (b) to increase the capacity of state and local institutions
to provide adequate services, particularly those which are production-related,
to the rural population. A special objective of the project would be the
development of a simple, relatively low-cost, participatory, and technically
sotnd approach to land redistribution and other land-related actions. This
aspect could have important consequences for rural development efforts
throughout Northeast Brazil.
Project Strategy
4.04      General.  In line with these objectives, project strategy during
the five-year investment period would focus on the elimination of specific
development constraints. The central element would be the improvement of
small farmer access to land and security of tenure, chiefly through a land
acquisition and redistribution program. This would be accompanied by a range
of activities aimed at increasing agricultural production and productivity and
providing certain basic physical and social infrastructure works. At the same
time, similar actions would take place involving low-income fishermen in the
project's coastal areas.
4.05      In order to reach intended beneficiaries, the project would also
focus upon increasing local and state institutional capabilities for rural
development. This institution-building strategy implies: (a) the creation of
new capabilities where none presently exist; (b) the strengthening of existing
capabilities with their specific channeling toward low-income farmers and
fishermen; (c) the promotion of new mechanisms for inter-institutional
coordination and program compatibility; and (d) the necessary involvement of
organized groups of beneficiaries in project planning and implementation.



- 12 -
4.06      Land-related Strategy.  While based on previous experiences, the
proposed project would be the first in Northeast Brazil to deal with land-
related issues in a comprehensive manner and on a significant scale. The
basic elements of the land-related strategy include:
(a)  The implementation of a diverse set of activities, including
land redistribution, discrimination 1/, and titling, to
increase small farmer access to land;
(b)  the utilization of a variety of methods, including purchase,
grants and expropriation, to acquire public and private lands
for subsequent redistribution;
(c)  the close linkage of land-related activities to agricultural
development activities and services; and
(d)  the establishment of a State Land Institute to deal effectively
with a range of land-related matters.
Additional principles guiding the project's land strategy, and land
redistribution in particular, include:
(e)  The minimization of population relocation; and
(f)  the adaptation of the land allotment process and land repayment
schemes to specific local conditions and beneficiary needs.
Project Design
4.07      As a result of the Government priority attached to the project,
land-related activities are already in early implementation stages. The
new state land institute (INTERPI) was legally created in December 1980, and
50,000 ha of land for redistribution under the project have already been
acquired by the Project Management Unit. Pre-allotment studies of this land
are currently under way. Since the project would be the first Bank-financed
rural development project in the state of Piaui and the first comprehensive
land project in Northeast Brazil, Bank financing and technical assistance
during the early stages of implementation could have an important impact
on project success. Consequently, project design and appraisal have placed
considerable emphasis on start-up mechanisms and procedures which would
be further refined as implementation proceeds.
1/ See footnote 1, page 3.



- 13 -
4.08      Due to the project's central focus on land-related activities,
efforts were also made to incorporate several special features into project
design. In order to ensure that complementary project actions remain
compatible with the scope and timing of land activities, a considerable
flexibility has been included in project design. While objectives, strategy,
general criteria and procedures, and detailed first-year implementation plans
have been elaborated for each project activity, detailed programming in each
subsequent year would be the result of an extensive annual planning exercise.
It is also expected that this type of project flexibility would allow for a
greater degree of effective beneficiary participation, particularly during the
planning stages. Annual planning, project flexibility, and the nature of the
land and agricultural development strategies increase the need for a strong
monitoring system capable of providing accurate and timely information to
project management.  Such a system has received special attention during
project preparation, and its effective functioning would be emphasized during
implementation.  The same is true for project evaluation.  Evaluation of
project impact would be necessary to re-orient and/or replicate project
design, particularly with respect to the project's principal objectives.  This
is especially important in view of the structural changes in land tenure which
are expected to occur.
Beneficiaries
4.09      Project activities would be directed to specific target groups
of low-income farmers and fishermen, including landless farmers (about 40,000
families), small owner-operators (about 8,000 families) with less than 100 ha,
and coastal fishermen (about 2,500 families). Of this total target population,
22%, or some 11,300 families, are expected to directly benefit from the
productive investments over the project's five-year investment period. This
includes (a) 5,000 farmers, the majority landless, to benefit from land
redistribution; (b) 3,000 small owner-operators; (c) 1,500 landless farmers
not involved in the initial land distribution program; and (d) 1,800 fishermen.
It is possible, however, that project land redistribution would benefit
a significantly larger number of beneficiaries due to the availability of
additional public land generated by discrimination activities. Similarly,
improved marketing and cooperative services, rural road improvements, and
water supply are expected to directly benefit a much larger percentage of
the target group population and of the rural population as a whole. Indirect
project benefits, derived from potential agricultural research results,
fishing and agricultural extension demonstration effects, and the staff
training and institution-building aspects of the project, could reach a
significant portion of the state's rural population. In addition, should the
project succeed in its objective of developing a simple, low-cost, technically
sound approach to land redistribution, experiences drawn from the project
could have an important impact on low-income farmers throughout Northeast
Brazil.



- 14 -
Project Activities
4.10      The project would include the following activities:
(a) Land-related activities, including acquisition of public
and private lands through a state-level land fund; land
redistribution; land discrimination; land titling services;
and assistance for the newly created State Land Institute.
(b) Agricultural development, including applied research, extension,
seed production, marketing, and small-scale irrigation.
(c) Fisheries development, including provision of equipment,
marketing facilities, and infrastructure; research; technical
assistance; and training.
(d) Physical and social infrastructure, including rehabilitation
and upgrading of existing rural roads, construction of new
rural access roads and tracks to properties, provision of water
supply facilities for selected communities, and improvements in
educational services and facilities.
(e) Project administration, including management, monitoring and
evaluation.
4.11      In support of project activities, credit for working capital
and investment credit for agricultural and fisheries development would be
provided by the Government (para 4.40-4.41). The project could also be
expected to encourage the channeling of public funds for other activities
into the project area. During project formulation, efforts were made to
avoid the substitution of POLONORDESTE financing for other available
sources of funds. Consequently, activities related to health are not
included in the project, and educational activities are limited to
extending coverage of the Bank-financed Northeast Basic Education Project
to the eleven project area municipalities currently not included in that
project.
B. Detailed Description
Land
4.12      Land-related activities form the core of the proposed project.
Implementation of most other project activities would be paced according
to the progress achieved in removing land constraints currently faced by
small farmers. During the five-year implementation period, the project
would: (a) acquire about 200,000 ha of public and private lands to be
redistributed to about 5,000 beneficiaries; (b) provide land discrimination
for abouft 9V,0OO ha; (c) develop titling services, benefiting about
1,500 small owners; and (d) assist in the establishment of a new state land



- 15 -
institute (INTERPI). In addition, assurances were obtained during negotia-
tions that the State Government would take all necessary actions to provide
the fishing communities with long-term tenure rights on federal coastal
lands. Implementation of land activities would be the responsibility of the
newly created INTERPI. The Land Unit (LU) within the project preparation
unit, which initiated project land activities, would be transferred to INTERPI.
INC'RA would provide technical assistance for all of the project's land
activities and particularly, for land discrimination. Other institutions,
such as EMATER-PI and FETAG (the State Federation of Agricultural Workers),
and local organizations would also be actively involved in the implementation
of project land activities. In addition, some 110 months of consultancy
services, employed under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, would
be financed by the project to provide technical assistance for land activities
(Annex 1) .
4.13      Land Acquisition.  Land would be acquired for subsequent redistribu-
tion through a variety of channels. Suitable public lands identified within
the project area would be transferred to INTERPI. Private lands would
be purchased at market prices through a project-specific land fund (FUNVALE).
In addition, other properties could be acquired for project use through
expropriation by INCRA. Various procedures prior to final acquisition,
incLuding agronomic evaluation and INCRA approval, would ensure that lands
were of a sufficient quality for small farmer agriculture and that purchase
prices would accurately reflect current market values (Annex 2). Some 50,000
ha have already been acquired for project use, and an additional 10,000 ha
have been identified for purchase. Thus far purchases have not stimulated
land price increases in the project area, and no significant land speculation
is anticipated (Annex 2). Developments in the land market would be closely
mon:itored throughout project execution (Annex 10). It is expected that by the
end of the project's first year, a cumulative total of at least 100,000 ha
would have been acquired, with at least 50,000 additional ha acquired during
each of the following two years. Since the pace of project implementation
as a whole would depend on the timely acquisition of land, assurances
were otained during negotiations that the State Government would take all
necessary steps to ensure that these minimum acquisition targets were met.
Although FUNVALE costs are included in total project costs, no disbursements
would be made against fund expenditures for land purchase.
4.14      Land Redistribution.  Subsequent to land acquisition, the
project would finance the costs of transitory management, feasibility
studies and other planning procedures for allotment, and demarcation and
titling costs. At least one year would be required from the date of acquisition
to the actual redistribution of the lands to project beneficiaries.
During this transitory period, various analyses of the property would be
carried out, including photo interpretation, selective soil and water
resources surveys, topographical studies, an infrastructure needs
assessment, and socio-economic surveys leading to a preliminary beneficiary
selection. Concurrently, teams of EMATER-PI extensionists would be
working among potential beneficiaries explaining project objectives and



- 16 -
land allotment and repayment schemes. Following this stage, a first allotment
plan would be drawn up by INTERPI, with the assistance of consultants and
other project staff. This would then be discussed with beneficiaries, and
once a final allotment plan had been agreed upon, it would be presented to
INCRA for approval. Demarcation and preliminary titling procedures could
follow. Each beneficiary would receive a plot ranging in size from about 35 ha
to 50 ha, defined in accordance with INCRA module size and local conditions.
It is expected that some 450 beneficiaries would have received land by the end
of the first project year.
4.15      Beneficiaries would be required to pay for the land received under
the project. Because of the limited availability of land purchase credit, a
simple Direct Repayment Plan, well suited to local traditions, has been
established. Under the plan, beneficiaries would pay for their land in rice
or an equivalent sum in cash in four annual installments. The total amount of
rice to be paid would be based on the land value, taking into account production
potential, infrastructure and access, determined during the allotment period.
Each annual installment would be equal to or less than the amount of rent
currently paid by area tenants. The rice would be collected in the field by
area cooperatives, processed and subsequently transferred to COBAL, which
would pay INTERPI. Where cooperatives do not exist, COBAL would carry out the
direct field collection (para 4.25-4.27). In the case of unfavorable climatic
conditions, repayments would be rescheduled, with no resulting loss to the
project, given the existing parity between inflation rates and increases in
the market price for rice.   Under the Direct Repayment Plan, it is expected
that 20 to 25 percent of real land acquisition and redistribution costs would
be recovered. Certain conditions, such as the acceptance of technical assistance
by EMATER-PI and the agreement not to sell, sublet, or allow sharecroppers on
any portion of the land, would be placed on beneficiary usage of the land
during the repayment period.
4.16      Land Discrimination and Titling.  In order to generate land for
subsequent redistribution and assist small owners in securing legal titles
to their land, the project would finance the discrimination of about one
third of the total project area during the second, third, and fourth project
years. The areas to be discriminated are currently being selected and would
be fully identified during the first project year. In each selected municipality,
all legal land claims would be identified, with public lands distinguished
from private holdings. Subsequently, suitable public lands would be turned
over to INTERPI for redistribution while privately owned lands would be
demarcated and titled as required.l/ The costs of these services, which vary
with property size, would be fully recovered, in accordance with INCRA
1/ The exact amount of public lands available for redistribution would be
known only after discrimination had taken place. A reasonable estimate,
based on experiences in other Northeast areas, would be about 90,000 ha
or ten percent of the discriminated area.



- 17 -
regulations. In the case of project beneficiaries, these services would be
initially supported by the project which would provide the necessary personnel,
equipment and facilities, with full cost recovery also in accordance with INCRA
regulations. The project would also support special legal assistance for small
owners involved in land discrimination. Three lawyers belonging to an independent
organization satisfactory to the Bank would be contracted beginning in the
second project year to work with INTERPI and assist small farmers in preparing
legal documents. About 1,500 small owners would benefit from these project
services, while the discrimination process as a whole would benefit much larger
numbers. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that: (a) by October 1,
1981, a complete program for land discrimination activities, including the
identification of the areas to be discriminated, would be forwarded to the Bank
for review and approval; and (b) by April 1, 1982, the lawyers to provide legal
assistance to smallholders would be contracted under terms and conditions
satisfactory to the Bank. In addition to eliminating land tenure insecurity,
it is expected that land discrimination would lead to a significant increase in
land tax revenues since, within each affected municipality, all owners and the
exact size of their properties would be registered. Furthermore, because of the
progressive nature of the tax with respect to size of holding and degree of
non-utilization, large owners would be provided with an incentive to either
sell part of their property to the project or to better utilize their land
resources.
4.17      State Land Institute.  In order to implement project activities
and increase state institutional capabilities in land-related matters, the
project would assist in the establishment of INTERPI. The project Land Unit,
which is currently being transferred to INTERPI, would constitute the core
staff of this new institute. Equipment, staff and other operational costs
directly related to project activities, including the establishment of
effective titling and cadastral services, would be financed under the project.
The project would also provide technical assistance for the establishment of
INTERPI by financing four senior consultants in the following areas:, technical
services; projects and operations; administration and finances; and recruitment
and training.   Assurances were obtained during negotiations that these
consultants would be employed, promptly as needed, on terms and conditions
satisfactory to the Bank (Annex 3, Table 2).
Agricultural Development
4.18      The project's agricultural development activities would be closely
linked to land-related activities and would be focused upon land beneficiaries,
other small owners with secure tenure, and some landless farmers. Adoption
rates have been estimated conservatively; it is expected that about 18% of the
total target group would apply some of the proposed recommendations. At the
same time that the land constraint to small farmer development would be
eliminated, applied research efforts would focus upon the adaptation of
technically sound recommendations to improve current farming practices.
Initial recommendations would be based on practices currently employed by more
advanced farmers in the project area. These practices, appropriate for a
large majority of the target group, are mainly related to increases in cropped
area and labor productivity through mechanization with animal traction.
Other possible improvements would include: (a) reductions in crop failure
due to drought conditions through increased soil permeability and water



- 18 -
storage capabilities; (b) increases in crop productivity through improved
planting materials, fertilizer and pesticide use, and timely planting; and
(c) diversification of crop alternatives, including improved intercropping
systems. The new techniques would be diffused by area extension agents, who
would already be well known to farmers through their participation in project
land activities. Improved seed production and distribution would ensure the
availability of suitable planting materials. Project marketing services
would guarantee small farmers necessary inputs and production outlets at
reasonable prices, while at the same time providing facilities for land
repayment. Finally, in order to develop the area's water resources for
agricultural purposes, the project would assist in designing and implementing
small-scale irrigation schemes for about 1,500 project beneficiaries.
4.19      Applied Research.  The applied research program, to be implemented
by EMBRAPA, would complement expected activities included in the proposed
national Agricultural Research II Project and would focus upon: (a) the
adaptation of improved seed varieties, chemical and organic fertilizers, plant
protection measures, and pasture improvements for target group farmers;
(b) the improvement and adaptation of farm implements and animal traction
equipment to local conditions; (c) the testing of new crop alternatives
(vegetables, sorghum, soybeans, groundnuts, etc.), new intercropping systems,
and new technical packages for irrigated lands; (d) the testing of various
alternatives for appropriate soil and water management; and (e) the setting up
of a permanent mechanism for continuous technology transfer from applied
research to extension. The research team would be comprised of a coordinator
and ten assistants, the majority of whom would be senior agriculturalists with
research and extension experience. In the area of small mechanization and
animal traction, the project research team would receive support from
specialists at the EMBRAPA regional experimental station (CPATSA -
Agricultural Research Center for the Semi-Arid Tropics) in the neighboring
state of Pernambuco. Each year, a research council, composed of farmers'
representatives, and representatives from EMATER-PI, EMBRAPA, CEPA-PI, and the
Project Management Unit would review the past year's research results, decide
upon the techniques ready for wider dissemination by extension, and approve the
research program for the following year.
4.20      During the five-year project investment period, a full program
of applied research would be carried out, with equipment, staff, and other
incremental operating costs financed by the project. Trials would be
conducted at four levels: (a) the central experimental station at Teresina;
(b) complementary sub-stations, to permit first adaptations to the Delta
do Parnaiba and Medio Parnaiba sub-areas; (c) farm level experimentation,
conducted with farmers' participation; and (d) demonstration fields cultivated
by the farmers themselves under EMATER-PI supervision. New techniques would
be thoroughly tested at these levels before release for mass diffusion
(Annex 3, Table 3).
4.21      Rural Extension.  Rural extension would be closely coordinated
with project land and applied research activities. Under the project, it
is expected that some 9,500 farm families would be assisted.  Both agricul-
cultural extension and social extension, with a primary empThasis on improved



- 19 -
nutrition, would be provided. In the areas of land redistribution, the
extension service would also play a key role in ensuring community partici-
pation both prior to and during the land redistribution process. To accom-
plish these goals, the institutional capacity of EMATER-PI, the executing
agency, would be considerably strengthened. This would include the construc-
tion and equipping of local offices in municipalities where offices currently
do not exist; improved training and equipment for local extensionists; and
additional staff at state, regional, and local levels. Farmer training
courses, equipment and materials for demonstration plots, including those
using animal traction, vehicles, and costs associated with extension through
the mass media would also be financed under the project.
4.22      During the first year of the project, about 2,000 small farmers
and their families would be attended by the extension service. This
includes 1,460 families previously assisted under the POLONORDESTE Delta
do Parnaiba project, 450 land beneficiaries and 90 small owners and
their families who would be assisted for the first time. Annual planning
for extension activities in subsequent years would take place in accordance
with the project's land activities. Extension methodology would emphasize
small group formation, mass media techniques and demonstration fields.
Agricultural and social extensionists would work in teams. Their tasks,
particularly among land beneficiaries, would include: (a) assisting in
socio-economic surveys of potential beneficiaries in areas where land has
been acquired by the project; (b) providing information, particularly on
land redistribution and repayment procedures and land discrimination, to
potential beneficiaries; (c) assisting in the selection of land beneficiaries
and in the subsequent allotment process; (d) providing training and technical
assistance for the utilization of improved agricultural practices, fertilizer,
pesticides, improved seeds, animal traction, adequate soil and water management,
marketing and farm management; (e) assisting farmers in obtaining official
bank credit, emphasizing group credit applications where possible; and (f)
providing social extension, particularly in the areas of improved nutrition
and hygiene, community organization, and cottage industries. Because of this
large task diversification, extension agents within each team would be
partially specialized and would concentrate on specific topics, such as
irrigation, animal traction, and land-related matters.
4.23      In order to enable EMATER-PI to carry out its work program under
the project, a project coordinator within EMATER-PI at the state level
would be appointed. The coordinator would be assisted by production and
social advisers. In addition, a number of specialists, including an account-
ing assistant, a training adviser, a marketing and cooperative specialist,
and an animal traction and small equipment expert, would be employed or
assigned to project activities. Additional staff, including marketing
specialists, would also be provided for the three regional EMATER-PI offices
in the project area. Terms of reference for key EMATER-PI project personnel
have been reviewed by the Bank and found to be satisfactory. By Year 4 of the
project, 79 additional agricultural extensionists and 51 additional social
extensionists would be employed by the project. While extension coverage
ratios would be initially high due to the added tasks associated with land
redistribution, ratios would be gradually decreased beginning in the fifth
year of the project until reaching a level of one agricultural extensionist
per 400 farmers and one social extensionist for every 600 farm families at
full development (Annex 3, Table 4).



- 20 -
4.24      Seed Production.  An improved state-wide seed production and
distribution system has been recently established. In addition to a 240
ha physical base for the production of basic seeds, Piaui now possesses
various seed processing facilities, three of which are located in the project
area, and a seed analysis laboratory located in Teresina. The project would
complement this newly created system to ensure adequate seed supply to project
beneficiaries. EMATER-PI, in conjunction with three area cooperatives, would
select a group of project area small farmers to multiply basic seeds under
close supervision. The cooperatives, utilizing advance contracts, would
purchase the seeds, which would be treated and stored in three project-
financed 1,000-ton seed storage facilities to be located on the cooperatives.
The cooperatives, through their input supply network (paras 4.25-4.27), would
also be responsible for the sale of the certified seeds to project farmers.
In order to encourage the use of the improved seeds, one of the major
recommendations proposed for improving crop technology, farmers would be
charged only about two thirds of market price for the seeds during the first
year of diffusion. The price would be progressively increased so that by the
end of the project execution period, all farmers would be paying full market
price for the improved seeds (Annex 3, Table 5).
4.25      Marketing.  Project marketing activities would increase small
farmer marketing options by strengthening and integrating public, private,
and cooperative actions and directing them towards the small farmer. Project
marketing activities include: (a) the provision of marketing information
through a preliminary study by CEPA-PI of the current marketing situation in
the project area and the expansion and strengthening of SIMA's marketing
information system; (b) the improvement and extension of CIDAPI-s and the
cooperatives' input sales network, including the opening of about eight
new input supply stores; (c) the collection, processing, and marketing of
small farmers' agricultural production, including the rice for land repayment,
to be undertaken by the cooperatives and COBAL; (d) the participation of local
retailers and cooperatives in COBAL's wholesale supply system (rede SOMAR) for
basic food items; and (e) the expansion of EMATER-PI and OCEPI technical
assistance and training programs in small farmer marketing and cooperative
development. In order to strengthen the involved institutions- planning and
implementation capacities, the project would provide for thirty months of
consultancy services in the first project year for CEPA-PI, SIMA, CIDAPI and
OCEPI. A project marketing coordinator within CEPA-PI would coordinate and
monitor all project marketing activities and ensure that the marketing needs
of land beneficiaries were being adequately met (Annex 8).
4.26      During the first year, CEPA-PI would complete the marketing study
which would guide project actions in subsequent years. SIMA would open a
new information agency in Sao Pedro de Piaui and would strengthen its
Teresina and Parnaiba agencies. Consultants would plan for the opening
of new CIDAPI input supply stores. On-farm collection of rice by coopera-
tives, with COBAL technical assistance, for land repayment would provide
the starting point for the collection, processing, and marketing of



- 21 -
beneficiary agricultural production in subsequent project years. COBAL,
during the first project year, would plan for implementation of the SOMAR
network and for its participation in project area rice marketing, including
the collection of rice for land repayment in those municipalities where
cooperatives do not presently exist. OCEPI, with a consultant's assistance,
would set up a centralized accounting system, and fiscal-legal and economic
planning advisory services for the five cooperatives participating in project
activities. Finally, three EMATER regional subject matter specialists in
storage and marketing would be financed and trained under the project. These
specialists would subsequently train extensionists in on-farm storage techniques
ancl small farmer and cooperative marketing.
4.27      During negotiations, assurances were obtained that:  (a) the detailed
out:line of the marketing study would be sent to the Bank for review prior to
the initiation of field work and the final version of the study would be
presented to the Bank by April 1, 1982; (b) the SIMA, CIDAPI, and OCEPI
consultants' final reports and other written material would be sent to
the Bank for review by October 1, 1981; (c) COBAL's first year feasibility
study and detailed plans for rice collection and marketing and the SOMAR
supply network would be sent to the Bank for review and approval by October 1,
1981; (d) a full-time EMATER-PI technician would be maintained in each of the
cooperatives participating in the project; (e) consultants would be employed
promptly as needed, under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank;
and (f) installation and operating plans for cooperative and CIDAPI input
supply stores and the improved input distribution network would be sent to the
Bank for review and approval prior to installation (Annex 3, Table 6).
4.28      Small-Scale Irrigation.  The project would assist in the development
of suitable, relatively low-cost approaches and the strengthening of state
institutional capacity for the implementation of small-scale irrigation
schemes. About 1,500 ha of irrigated lands for about 1,500 land redistribution
beneficiaries would be developed by private firms contracted and supervised
by INTFRPI. A State Water Commission, comprised of representatives from the
Secretariat of Agriculture, EMATER, DNOCS, and INTERPI would be set up to
establish water charges and review feasibility studies and designs. Local
water users associations would be responsible for the collection of user
charges and operation and maintenance of the systems.
4.29      Irrigation schemes would be chosen according to a set of technical,
economic, and social criteria, including water availability, soil quality,
profitability, and population density (Annex 2). In the floodplains along the
Parnaiba River, the schemes would mainly consist of protective dikes and small
pumping stations. In a limited number of cases, irrigation would also be
developed inland through tubewells and pumps for underground water. Irrigation
potential on properties already acquired by INTERPI is currently being assessed.
4.30      Feasibility studies carried out by consultants would be sent tco the
Bank for approval prior to the completion of detailed designs and construction
of the schemes. Assurances on this matter were obtained during negotiations.
Assurances were also obtained that: (a) consultants would be employed under



- 22 -
terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank; (b) the State Water Commission
would be legally established no later than October 1, 1981; (c) a water users
association would be set up prior to the construction of each scheme; and
(d) before the completion of the works for each irrigation scheme, the State
Water Commission would establish a water charge scheme, satisfactory to the
Bank, providing for the full recovery of operating and maintenance costs and
the recovery of investments in infrastructure and equipment, taking into
account the beneficiaries' repayment capacity. It is expected that investment
cost per hectare would not exceed US$5,000 and that charges, while based on
federal guidelines, would reflect the rate of inflation and farmers' ability
to pay. Estimated investment cost recovery would average about 30% to 35%
(Annex 2; Annex 3, Table 7).
Fisheries Development
4.31      The project's coastal fisheries development activities would seek
to remove production, marketing and organizational constraints currently
facing small fishermen. The proposed intervention, the first of its kind to
be supported by the Bank in Northeast Brazil, would strengthen the artisanal
fishing sector by increasing the availability of known technology already
employed to a limited extent within the project area, and by improving
existing institutional arrangements. The project would provide funds for:
(a) extension, technical assistance and training to be implemented by EMATER-PI
and OCEPI; (b) experimentation and research to be carried out by SUDEPE; and
(c) coordination of project activities to be carried out by SUDEPE with the
assistance of outside consultants. In addition, not financed by the Bank, but
essential to the success of project fishing activities, the official banking
system, utilizing standard POLONORDESTE credit procedures, would provide:
(a) credit to fishermen for fishing vessels, including an estimated 40 canoes,
30 traditional sailboats, 110 improved sailboats, and 50 motorized sailboats;
(b) credit to fishermen for engines, safety equipment, and fishing gear; c)
credit to merchants and shrimp and mangrove crab processors, for tools,
equipment, and working capital; and (d) credit to boatbuilders for equipment
and working capital. It is expected that these activities would benefit about
1,800 fishermen over the five-year investment period.
4.32      Extension and technical assistance would focus on the transfer of
technologies related to the income-generating activities of the fishing
family. In addition to introducing new fishing techniques, the training
of family members in net making and repair, fish conservation, nutrition,
and the production of food and artisanal items would take place. A small
revolving fund would be set up by EMATER-PI to finance such activities by
small groups. It is expected that about 345 fishermen and 80 families would
be assisted by EMATER-PI's fishing technicians and social extensionists during
the first year. Technical assistance would also be provided to the Luis
Correia cooperative. Administrative and technical personnel currently
financed by the Delta do Parnaiba POLONORDESTE project would continue under
the proposed project. In addition, OCEPI would provide technical assistance
for administration, auditing, accounting, marketing and legal matters (paras
4.25-4.26).
4.33      Experimentation and research under the project would test new
types of fishing gear and equipment, as well as different types of fishing
systems. Existing knowledge about the area's marine resources would be
improved. For these purposes, personnel and equipment, including a small



- 23 -
vessel and the operating costs of the SUDENE research ship, would be financed
by the project. In addition, consultancy services would be provided for
experiments with different types of fishing gear, a fish stock assessment,
marketing studies, shipbuilding and for improving the evaluation system of
extension and training. Fifteen man-months of consultancies would be financed
during the first year of the project. Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that consultants would be employed promptly as needed under terms
and conditions satisfactory to be Bank. In order to coordinate the actions of
the various institutions involved in project fishing activities, a project
fishing coordinator would be appointed to SUDEPE (Annex 3, Table 8).
Physical and Social Infrastructure
4.34      Rural Roads.  Proposed road improvements under the project include:
(a) the design and construction of about 400 km of new access roads, including
rural tracks within properties to be redistributed; and (b) minimum road
improvements for about 1,350 km of existing roads. To carry out this program
and ensure the adequate maintenance of the municipal road system, the project
would also include: (a) consultant services for the preparation of engineer-
ing designs and bidding documents, and for the supervision of civil works;
(b) equipment, spare parts, and hand tools for municipal road maintenance;
(c) a small soils laboratory to support monitoring of road construction
quality; (d) training for municipal foremen in the implementation of basic
road maintenance; and (e) personnel to assist municipal foremen in the
design and execution of municipal road maintenance. An infrastructure sub-
coordinator within the Project Management Unit would coordinate and monitor
road activities under the project. The coordinator would be assisted by an
engineer who would review and approve design work, bidding documents, and
bidding evaluations. CODERPI, mainly through the use of consultants and
contractors, would be responsible for road construction and improvements.
DFEE-PI, by providing maintenance equipment, training, and technical personnel,
wotld assist the municipalities in adequately maintaining project roads.
4.35      During the first project year, some 258 km of roads would be improved
within the municipalities of Batalha, Regeneracao, Palmerais, and Luis Correia.
Based upon the progress achieved and the requirements of the project's land
activities, annual work programs, including lists of roads to be upgraded or
constructed during each subsequent year, would be presented to the Bank for
review and comments no later than October 1. Assurances were obtained on this
matter during negotiations. Assurances were also obtained that: (a) CODERPI
would prepare engineering designs and bidding documents, award construction
contracts, and supervise construction, all in accordance with POLONORDESTE
standards previously agreed upon with the Bank; (b) CODERPI would provide to
the Bank for review and comment the list of consultants engaged for road
design and CODERPI supervision staffing plans by October 1 of each year;
(c) consultants would be employed whose qualifications and experience were
satisfactory to the Bank; (d) DER-PI would provide mechanized maintenance
assistance to municipalities in the project area in accordance with appropriate
engineering and mechanical standards; (e) a convenio would be established
between each municipality and DER-PI, outlining respective responsibilities
for road rehabilitation and maintenance, prior to the initiation of civil



- 24 -
works, and an annual work program would be jointly prepared and carried out
for road maintenance of previously improved municipal roads; (f) DER-PI would
provide adequate workshop support to operate and maintain equipment purchased
under the project; and (g) all equipment procured under the project would be
used solely for the maintenance of state and municipal roads in the project
area (Annex 3, Table 9).
4.36      Water Supply.  Under the project, some 200 low-cost water supply
systems, including public laundries, would be constructed and some 11,300
water filters distributed, benefiting a total population of some 65,000
persons. FSESP would be responsible for all preinvestment and special studies,
planning and design of the systems, construction supervision, and quarterly
water analyses once systems were operational. CIDAPI would drill all necessary
wells, construct or contract the construction of the systems and laundries,
train local operators, and carry out maintenance backstopping. EMATER-PI
would distribute water filters and instruct villagers on proper water use. The
municipalities would be responsible for operating costs and the collection of
water charges.
4.37      During the first year of the project, institutional capacity
would be strengthened and twenty water supply systems would be built in
municipalities where project land and fisheries activities would be taking
place. Each system would be composed of: a water source; a hand or motor-
driven pump, depending on community size and availability of electricity;
a pump house; an elevated storage tank; public hydrants; and connections
to existing schools and health posts. Prior to construction of each system
and accompanying laundry, a pre-investment study would be carried out and a
convenio would be concluded among FSESP, CIDAPI, and the municipalities.
Feasibility and pre-investment studies would be carried out in accordance with
satisfactory federal guidelines established for the PIASS program (Program for
Hlealth and Sanitation in Rural Areas). In order to ensure proper operation
and maintenance of the systems, a new water supply division would be created
in CIDAPI. Assurances were obtained on these matters during negotiations.
During the project investment period, user charges would cover only local
operating costs, but would be gradually raised from the sixth year on to cover
all system operating costs (Annex 3, Table 10).
4.38      Education.  The project would extend the educational activities
included in the Northeast Basic Education Project 1/ to the eleven project
municipalities which were not included in that project. The State Secretariat
of Fducation would be the executing agency. In addition to curriculum reform,
provision of educational materials and teacher training, schools would be
built or renovated and equipped in those muncipalities where project land
redistribution and fishing activities were taking place. During the first
project year, no new educational activities would begin; however, personnel
and ongoing primary school construction previously provided for under the
POLONORDESTE Delta do Parnaiba project would be financed under the proposed
project (Annex 3, Table 11).
l/ See Brazil, Northeast Basic Education Project, Stati ,      taiae  Wesot
No. 2815-BR, dated April 28, 1980.



- 25 -
Project Administration
4.39      The project would be one of six ongoing rural development projects
coordinated by the State POLONORDESTE Technical Unit (TU) which is linked
to the State Secretariat of Planning. A separate Project Management Unit
(PMU) would be set up within the Technical Unit to coordinate project activities.
At the central level, the PMU would consist of a Project Coordinator; three
technical sub-coordinators for land, agricultural development, and physical
and social infrastructure; two full-time staff for financial control and
reporting; a Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation Sub-Unit with three full-time
professionals; and appropriate support staff. In addition, three regional
field coordinators, one for each of the project's sub-areas, would be responsible
for daily project coordination at the sub-area and local levels. The project
would finance equipment and vehicles, PMU staff salaries at competitive
levels, travel expenses, consultant fees for monitoring and evaluation, and
other incremental operating costs for project coordination, monitoring and
evaluation within the TU. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
that: (a) the PMU would be established and maintained under conditions
satisfactory to the Bank; (b) the qualifications and experience and terms and
conditions of employment of the Project Coordinator would be satisfactory to
the Bank; and (c) the terms and conditions of employment for consultants to
be contracted for evaluation studies would be satisfactory to the Bank (paras
6.01-6.08; Annex 3, Table 12).
Credit Provision
4.40      Due to the continuation of highly negative real interest rates for
rural credit in Brazil, credit would not be included in Bank financing for
the proposed project. Credit provision, however, would be an essential
complement to project activities. The various types of credit to be provided
include:
(a) Seasonal Short-Term Agricultural Credit to finance inputs
and other costs associated with the production of short
cycle crops; the establishment of semi-permanent crops; and
the maintenance and improvement of established permanent
crops. 1/
(b) Medium and Long-Term Agricultural Credit for land clearing,
livestock, storage facilities, small irrigation schemes,
animal traction, farm tools and equipment, and other on-farm
development costs.
(c) Medium and Long-Term Fisheries Credit for vessels, gear,
and equipment for fishermen, merchants, processors, ship-
builders, and the fishing cooperative.
(d) Short-Term Credit for working capital for the fishing
cooperative.
1/   Under present credit regulations, up to seventy-five percent of total
production costs, including labor, can be financed through credit
obtained from the official banks.



- 26 -
Incremental credit requirements for the five year project execution period are
estimated at about US$23.3 million equivalent, of which 35% would be incremental
short-term agricultural credit, 50% incremental medium and long-term agricultural
credit, and 15% investment and working capital credit for fishing activities.
Some short-term marketing credit and some investment and working capital
