credit for agricultural cooperatives would also be made available through
existing credit lines.
4.41      Credit for project-related activities would be provided by the
Federal Government under its current POLONORDESTE credit line or through
its "normal" rural credit program for Northeast Brazil. Participating
official banks include: the Banco do Brasil (BB), the Banco do Nordeste
do Brasil (BNB), the Banco Nacional de Credito Cooperativo (BNCC), and the
State bank, the Banco do Estado de Piaui (BEP). In order to improve the
access of project beneficiaries to the official banking system, additional
credit posts required to adequately serve the project area would be identified
during the annual planning process (para 6.04). During negotiations,
assurances would be sought from the Government that it would take all necessary
steps to ensure the availability of adequate credit, as defined during the
annual planning exercise, for project participants.
V. PROJECT COST AND FINANCING
Project Cost
5.01      Total project costs over the five-year project investment period
(mid-1981 to mid-1986) are estimated at US$84.4 million, including a foreign
exchange cost of US$16.3 million or about 19% and taxes of about US$1.7
million. Baseline costs were estimated at appraisal (September 1980) prices.
Physical contingencies total 6% of baseline costs, and price contingencies
are equivalent to 27% of baseline costs. Physical contingencies were added to
baseline costs at 20% for irrigation and water supply construction, 15% for
other construction, and 5% for all equipment, vehicles, and vehicle operation
and maintenance. Price contingencies were calculated in US dollar terms,
based on international inflation rates estimated at 12.5% for 1980, 9.0% for
1981, 8.5% for 1982, 8.0% for 1983, 7.5% for 1984, and 7.0% thereafter.
Project costs are summarized below and annual phasing of investments is shown
in Annex 3, Table 1.

Financing
5.02      The proposed Bank loan of US$29 million to the Federative Republic
of Brazil would finance 35% of total project costs net of taxes. This would
include the total foreign exchange component of the project plus about US$13
million equivalent of local costs. The loan would be for 15 years, including
3 years of grace. The balance of project financing would be provided by the
Federal Government through the POLONORDESTE program. In addition, the Federal
and State Governments would finance agricultural and fisheries credit (about
US$23 million) and essential complementary items, such as teacher salaries
and additional roads, not included in project costs financed by the Bank.
During negotiations assurances were obtained from the State and Federal
Governments that adequate counterpart funding would be available for the
effective and timely execution of the project and that project facilities and
services would be adequately operated and maintained. Assurances were also
obtained from the State that it would arrange for the timely acquisition
and necessary financing of additional land or rights-of-way required for
project works.
5.03      To ensure timely and effective project implementation, various
start-up activities, including those related to land acquisition, staffing,
and employment of consultants, have already been initiated or would be
initiated during the first half of 1981. Retroactive financing of up to
US$2,000,000 equivalent is proposed to help cover eligible expenditures made
after October 1, 1980, but before loan signature.
Procurement
5.04      Most of the equipment, materials and vehicles required by the
project are locally produced and readily available. Such items would be
procured in accordance with local procurement procedures which are acceptable.
Civil works and consultancy services (totalling US$7.1 million) for rural road
improvement and construction would be procured following state competitive
bidding procedures. Although these procedures do not exclude foreign firms,
construction lots would be sized to allow bids from small and medium
contractors, and it is expected that all civil work and consultancy contracts
for roads would be awarded to local firms. Road maintenance equipment would
be acquired in two lots on the basis of local competitive bidding with national
advertising. Hand tools would be procured through local suppliers in accordance
with state competitive bidding procedures. Most civil works for water supply
systems, small-scale irrigation, marketing, and other project construction
(about US$8.3 million) would be awarded on the basis of competitive bidding
advertised locally, although a small portion of these works (mainly for water
supply) could be carried out by force account. Brazil has a competitive local
construction industry which is capable of carrying out project works. Since
the individual construction works are relatively small and quite dispersed,
foreign contractors are not expected to be interested, although they would not
be excluded from bidding. About 233 months of short-term consultancy services,
to provide technical assistance and training in new activities related to
lana, fise-t es, irrirgation, marketing, extension, monitoring ana evaluation,
would be mainly procured locally, at an average cost of about US$5,000 per
month.



- 29 -
Disbursements
5.05      The proceeds of the Bank loan would be disbursed on the basis of
an overall rate of 35% financing of total project cost net of taxes during
the project investment period. This would result in a 36% rate of disburse-
ment for each project component excluding the land fund. Disbursements would
be made to the Central Bank of Brazil against withdrawal applications covering
statements of expenditures initiated by the various implementing agencies and
certified by the PMU. In order to reduce the interval during which the
Government would finance the Bank's share of project costs with its own
resources, a Special Account would be opened in the Central Bank with an
initial deposit of up to US$2,000,000. The Government may request the Bank to
make advance payments in dollars from the loan account into this Special
Account. Withdrawals in cruzeiros from the account would be made on the basis
of the statements of expenditures forwarded to the Central Bank. Conversion
from dollars into cruzeiros would be made at the exchange rate that prevailed
on the date the expenditures were made. The Central Bank would send the
statements to the Bank, which would then replenish the Special Account.
Supporting documentation for salaries, administrative expenses and minor
construction under force account would not be submitted to the Bank, but would
be retained by the PMU and made available for inspection by the Bank during
project supervision missions. Standard documentation covering civil works,
vehicles, equipment, and technical assistance would be submitted to the Bank.
Disbursements are expected to occur over about five years. The estimated
disbursement schedule is shown in Annex 11.
VI. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
Organization and Management
6.01      The proposed project would be part of the POLONORDESTE program
and organizational framework within the state of Piaui. A POLONORDESTE
State Management Council, chaired by the Governor and comprised of the heads
of all state secretariats and official banks and representatives from the
state rural trade union, the state university, and the Federal Ministry of
Agriculture, meets regularly to decide policy issues and give overall guidance
to the state's rural development activities. Important operational issues
involving POLONORDESTE projects are dealt with by an Implementation Committee,
comprised of executive staff from all of the implementing agencies. This
committee, which meets monthly, is chaired by the coordinator of the State
POLONORDESTE Technical Unit (TU). The TU, administratively part of the
planning foundation (CEPRO) linked to the Secretariat of Planning, is
responsible for the daily coordination, administration, monitoring, and
evaluation of all state POLONORDESTE projects. The TU would be reinforced
under the proposed project, chiefly through the creation of a special Project
Management Unit (PMU) to coordinate project activities. To facilitate project
communication, one senior staff member would be designated within each
executing agency to serve as a link with the PMU (Annex 9).  A list of
executing agencies and respective implementation responsibilities under the
project is shown in Annex 7.



- 30 -
6.02      The PMU, headed by a Project Coordinator, would be responsible
for the overall coordination, monitoring, and evaluation of project activities.
It would be comprised of: (a) three sub-coordinators to coordinate and
monitor project land, agriculture development, and infrastructure activities;
(b) a Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation Sub-Unit, with three full-time
professionals, to keep up-to-date records on physical implementation progress,
conduct short-term studies on specific implementation problems, contract
and supervise major evaluation studies, consolidate annual work programs and
budgets, and generally manage the project's monitoring and evaluation systems
(paras 6.05-6.06); (c) Administrative and Financial Sub-Units, with two
full-time professionals and appropriate support staff; and (d) three regional
field coordinators to supervise and coordinate activities at the sub-area and
local levels. The PMU would be assisted in its coordinating task by a project
marketing sub-coordinator within CEPA-PI and a project fisheries sub-coordinator
within SUDEPE, both of whom would have direct access to the Project Coordinator.
The sub-coordinators within the PMU, CEPA-PI and SUDEPE would make regular
field visits and maintain daily contact with the executing agencies. They
would keep the Project Coordinator informed about implementation progress and
potential and actual problems or delays. The Project Coordinator would work
with the heads of the executing agencies to resolve any major difficulties.
Additional technical support for project activities would be available from
existing specialists within the TU (Annex 9).
Beneficiary Participation
6.03      The regional field coordinators, in conjunction with area extension
staff, would organize monthly meetings at the local and regional levels.
Participants would include project beneficiaries or their representatives,
executing agencies' field staff, local rural union representatives, and
PMU or other state-level staff as required. The meetings would serve several
purposes. They would: (a) provide beneficiaries with information on project
strategy, objectives, and activities; (b) provide feedback to field staff
and project management on beneficiary response to implementation; (c) provide
a forum for beneficiary and staff participation in decisions related to
property allotment and annual project planning; and (d) provide a formal
structure through which beneficiaries and local staff could discuss and
agree upon their respective responsibilities under the project. Meetings
would be scheduled to allow for maximum attendance, and whenever possible,
problems would be solved at the local level.
Annual Planning
6.04      Comprehensive annual planning would be essential to project success.
Early in each fiscal year, the Planning, Monitoring and Evaluation Sub-Unit
within the PMU would begin preliminary planning, including cost estimates, for
the following year's activities. From about June 15 to September 1, each
participating agency, with assistance from the PMU, would prepare an annual
plan detailing its proposed activities and expected project expenditures
during the next POLONORDESTE fiscal year. The PMU's Planning, Monitoring, and
Evaluation Sub-\nlt vould the'n consolidate thae planas and -istee thear overall
consistency. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the proposed
annual operating plans would be submitted by the PMU to the Bank no later than
October 1 of each year for review. These annual plans would be satisfactory
to the Bank. Assurances were also obtained that the Bank would be given a
reasonable opportunity to comment on any substantial modifications in those
plans as a result of their subsequent review by POLONORDESTE management and
the Federal Government.



- 31 -
Monitoring
6.05      The project monitoring system, to be managed by the Planning, Monitoring,
and Evaluation Sub-Unit within the PMU, would produce the information required
by management for decision-making and annual planning (para 6.04). The basic
information for monitoring would be provided by PMIU staff, the executing
agencies, and project beneficiaries. The five technical sub-coordinators and
the three regional field coordinators would continuously monitor implementation
progress for each component and advise the Project Coordinator on achievements
as well as shortfalls in project execution. Standardized forms would be
completed quarterly by the executing agencies, forwarded to the PMU, checked
with other available information, and consolidated into quarterly project
reports. These reports would be distributed to PMU and TU management and
project staff, the executing agencies, the POLONORDESTE State Management
Council, and Begional and Federal officials. The reports would give details
on project implementation progress and expenditures, including comparisons of
actual performance with targets, estimated costs, and key indicators. In
addition, information on a limited number of key indicators would be collected
monthly from a very small sample of beneficiaries in order to quantitatively
assess beneficiary response to project services and recommendations. Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that: (a) The PMU would complete ongoing
work with the executing agencies on standardized information requirements and
reporting formats; establish data analysis procedures; and provide a report
outlining these procedures to the Bank for review prior to their implementation,
but no later than October 1, 1981; and (b) the quarterly monitoring reports
would be provided to the Bank promptly upon their completion, but no later
than three months after the close of each POLONORDESTE fiscal quarter (Annex 10).
Evaluation
6.06      To complement monitoring activities, evaluation would aim to provide
project management and the State with the necessary information to adequately
plan project activities and to adjust, when necessary, project strategy and
land and rural development policies. Evaluation would be comprised of the
following activities: (a) ongoing evaluation; (b) a mid-term evaluation;
and (c) a post-project evaluation. Ongoing evaluation, carried out by the
Planning, Monitoring, and Evaluation Sub-Unit in the PMU, would consist of
short-term studies of specific project activities and implementation problems.
These studies would complement monitoring information and would be useful for
project management and annual planning. An in-depth mid-term evaluation would
be conducted during the third year to provide project management with information
on emerging development trends within the project area and preliminary assessments
of project impact both on beneficiaries and non-beneficiaries. Although the
PMU's Planning, Monitoring, and Fvaluation Sub-Unit would participate, the
mid-term study, as well as the ex-post evaluation, would be contracted externally
to consulting firms and/or academic institutions. During negotiations, assurances
were obtained that: (a) short-term studies would be sent to the Bank promptly
upon their completion, and those requiring more than six staff-months would be
subject to prior approval by the Bank; and (b) the project mid-term evaluation
report would be provided to the Bank no later than October 1, 1984 (Annex 10).
Operating Agreements
6.07      Consistent with a commonly used system in Brazil and to help
ensure effective inter-institutional coordination, a series of written
agreements (convenios) would be entered into. Three types of agreements
would be concluded: (a) convenios between the State Government through



- 32 -
SEPLAN and any executing agency which is: (i) not directly part of, or
responsible to, the State Government; (ii) largely autonomous; or
(iii) encharged with special responsibilities or the carrying out of new
procedures under the project; (b) convenios among executing agencies, with
the participation of the PMU, when more than one agency is involved in
executing a project activity (e.g. marketing, fisheries) or when two or more
project activities require particularly close coordination (e.g. research and
extension); and (c) convenios between executing agencies and involved munici-
palities or farmer groups prior to the construction of civil works. All of
these agreements would specify each party's obligations and responsibilities
under the project. Draft convenios involving the State Government and the
executing agencies and samples of convenios between agencies and local
entities would be reviewed during negotiations. Assurances were obtained
from the State Government that it would enter into and maintain all such
agreements during the project implementation period. A list of inter-agency
convenios which would be entered into is shown in Annex 6. Because of the
importance of project land activities, the receipt by the Bank of satisfactory
signed convenios between the State Government and INTERPI and between the
State Government and INCRA would be a condition of loan effectiveness.
Accounts and Auditing
6.08      Each of the participating agencies, including the PMU, would
maintain accounts of its project expenditures, which would be annually
audited by Government auditors according to satisfactory standard procedures
for POLONORDESTE projects. The PMU would retain all project statements of
expenditures. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that copies of
the audited statements of the various agencies' project accounts would be
provided to the Bank through the PMIU within six months of the end of the
POLONORDESTE fiscal year. Assurances were also obtained that these reports
would contain a specific reference as to the adequacy of the procedures used
for the verification and preparation of statements of expenditure for dis-
bursement requests.
VII. PRODUCTION, DEMAND, MARKETING, AND PRICES
Production
7.01      Production increases expected under the project would be achieved
through improvements in both agricultural and fisheries production systems.
In agriculture, increases in cultivated area and land and labor productivity
would be brought about by improved tenure security and small farmer access to
land, accompanied by the use of animal traction, some irrigation, and better
agricultural and managerial practices. This would be supported by a greater
access to credit, improved seeds and other inputs; an intensification of
research and extension efforts; and an improved marketing system. Increases
in fish production would come about as a result of a series of interventions
to improve catch levels, decrease losses, and increase the number of fishing



- 33 -
days. Improved practices and technology for fishing and fish processing would
be supported by the increased availability of credit; stronger research,
training, and extension; and expanded market opportunities. Estimated at
September 1980 farmgate and dock prices, the annual incremental value of
production at full development (Year 10) would total about US$34.3 million:
US$27.1 million for crops, US$4.9 million for livestock, and US$2.3 million
for fish (Table 7.1).
7.02      Cropping patterns are expected to remain relatively stable under
the project, and the traditional crops of rice, corn, beans, and manioc
would continue to predominate. Some changes, however, are expected to occur.
Livestock raising would be considerably expanded, particularly among currently
landless farmers who would be encouraged to incorporate pastureland into an
improved crop rotation fallow system (Annex 2). Some small-scale irrigation
beneficiaries would experiment with new crops, including fruits and vegetables,
and a few technically advanced farmers would be expected to progressively move
toward rice specialization. Yield increases have been conservatively estimated
on the basis of yields already obtained by more advanced small farmers, and
take into account annual variations. These variations are expected to
decline under the project, however, through the introduction of effective
water and soil management techniques.
7.03      The types and varieties of fish caught by project fishermen would
not undergo significant changes. Extension emphasis, however, would, be
placed on those species which could be readily processed and marketed. It
is expected that boatowners and fishermen, depending on their level of
technology adoption, would progressively catch greater quantities of higher
qual:ity fish. As in agriculture, yield estimates are relatively conservative
and do not exceed those currently obtained by the most progressive fishermen
in the project area. Actual production increases achieved would depend,
however, on the rate of increase in boat ownership and technology adoption,
and on fishermen's willingness to fish an increased number of days per year.
Estimates of these factors have been made taking into account the existing
socio-cultural environment and the expected improved quality of extension
efforts under the project.
Demand, Marketing, and Prices
7.04      Incremental project production would not be subject to demand
constraints. It is expected that all of the incremental bean and corn
production would be consumed locally, in most cases by the farm families
themselves. The state of Piaui in recent years has been a net exporter of
manioc flour and rice, two of the project's principal crops. The incremental
production of these products would be readily absorbed by the regional market,
either through traditional marketing channels or the COBAL distribution
network. Incremental livestock production would partially meet the existin6
state deficit for meat and dairy products. Recent surveys indicate that fish
consumption is rapidly increasing throughout the Northeast. While it is
expected that much of the project-s incremental fish production would be
used to satisfy demand in Teresina and the rest of the project area, some
production, particularly in processed form, would be exported to neighboring
states. A first-year marketing study would identify more precisely current
product flows, potential demand trends, and advantageous marketing
opportunities (paras 4.25-4.27).

7.05      Project actions were designed to improve small farmer and fishermen
marketing opportunities, allowing them to maintain or increase their share
of the final demand price. Improvements in the road system (para 4.34),
rural extension (para 4.21), cooperative and public marketing capabilities
(para 4.25), and marketing information systems (para 4.25) would increase
small producers' bargaining power and ensure a competitive climate and adequate
marketing outlets for the project-s principal products. While currently
producers- share of retail price varies from about 45% (manioc) to 75% (beans),
some increases in this share could be expected to occur as a result of project
marketing activities. No modifications are expected in the existing consumer
price structure, however.
VIII. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
Production Models
8.01      Based on differences in tenure situation, level of technology
adoption, use of irrigation, and degree of mechanization, ten farm models and
three boat models were analyzed. These models should be considered purely as
illustrations of possible agricultural and fisheries development, rather than
as firm estimates or guides for project activities. It is expected that by
full development in year ten, most farmers would be using some combination of
manual labor and animal traction, with an increased and more efficient use of
inputs and improved managerial practices. Similarly, for fisheries, most
fishermen would be utilizing intermediate levels of technology--improved
sailboats and fishing gear--with greater skill. The agricultural development
strategy is described in detail in Annex 2, and a brief summary of the agricul-
tural models by type of beneficiary is shown in Table 8.1. A detailed
analysis of selected farm and boat models and a summary of the boat models are
presented in Annex 4.
8.02      To illustrate on-farm and fishing operations, financial analysis
projections were estimated for all models. Prices of inputs, labor, and
production were assumed to be those prevailing in September 1980 at the
farmgate and beach-landing points. In general, for land beneficiaries the
financial rates of return of the models are high, averaging from about 80% to
85%. This mainly reflects farmers increased use of credit and technical
assistance once barriers imposed by insecure land tenure and lack of access to
land are removed. Both the integration of livestock production into the mixed
cropping/ livestock production system and the use of improved water retention
and soil management practices would help to stabilize the currently high
annual output and income variations induced by climatic conditions. In
fisheries, the financial rates of return reflect the relatively rapid and
substantial impact that simple technology improvements, increased boat
ownership, and better information and skills could have on target group
fishermen.

Producer Incomes
8.03      Agriculture.  Substantial income gains are expected to accrue to
project beneficiaries. These benefits would mainly result from: (a) secure
access to land in adequate quantities; (b) labor and crop productivity
increases; (c) a reduction in crop losses due to drought conditions; and
(d) improved marketing opportunities. Without the project, the family incomes
derived from agricultural activities of a typical landless farmer and small
owner have been estimated to average about US$900 and US$1,800, respectively.
Under the project, it is expected that average family income for new landowners
would increase to approximately US$3,750 at full development (Year 10). For
those new landowners with a small plot of irrigated land, average family
income would be about 10% higher while total plot size would be 25% smaller.
Average family incomes of current small owners would increase to about US$3,900
and those of landless farmers to about US$1,250. The expected development of
family farm income is summarized in Table 8.2.
8.04      Fisheries.  Major income benefits would be received by project
fishing families. It is expected that these benefits would mainly be derived
from: (a) a substantial increase in the number of effective fishing days;
(b) increased ownership and use of improved boats and gear; (c) better
information and fishing practices; (d) increased family and locally based
processing activities; and (e) improved marketing channels. Without the
project, average income per fishing family has been estimated at US$600 per
annum. It is expected that as a result of the project, average annual family
income would rise to approximately US$3,000. This average would be subject to
considerable variation among individual fishermen; smaller income increases
would be achieved by those fishing families who would not own boats (Annex 4).
IX.  ECONOMIC BENEFITS ATD JUSTIFICATION
Economic Rate of Return
9.01      The economic rate of return for the directly productive aspects
of the project has been estimated at about 20% (para 9.02). However, given
the number of variables involved, a range from about 15% to 25% might be a
more realistic estimate. For various aspects of the project, such as water
supply, education, and some rural roads, which would have significant benefits
not readily quantifiable, no specific economic rate of return was calculated.
Details of the cost and benefit streams used in the economic analysis are
given in Annex 5. Other details of the analysis are available in the project
file.
9.02      The economic prices of traded outputs and inputs were derived
from the IBRD projected world market prices expressed in 1980 US dollar
values, with appropriate adjustments for freight, handling, and processing.
The projected economic prices for non-traded items were derived by applying
conversion factors used in similar Bank projects in Northeastern Brazil to the
financial prices. A second set of economic prices was also obtained utilizing
project-specific conversion factors based on recent recalculations by AGREP.
rJnder those assumptions, the project's estimated economic rate of return would
rise to 26%. The financial prices of various commodities were projected on
the basis of historical price trends (Annex 5, Table 1).

- 39 -
9.03      The cost stream used in estimating the economic rate of return
included all productive investment costs directly benefiting the project
target group, some non-productive investment costs, and incremental operating
costs. This included: 100% of project costs (Bank and non-Bank-financed
baseline costs plus physical contingencies) for land activities excluding
discrimination, extension, small-scale irrigation, fisheries, and project
administration, monitoring, and evaluation; 80% of project costs for rural
roads; 70% of project costs for water supply; and 50% of project costs for
research, seed production, marketing, and education. All recurrent costs
needed to maintain project activities have been included for the full life
of the project.
9.04      Not all project benefits can be accounted for by the incremental
production of the 9,500 farmers and 1,800 fishermen directly assisted.
Increases would also be achieved by other rural producers as a result of
strengthened research and seed production efforts and the demonstration
effect of extension activities. Improved marketing services could provide
increased production incentives for many of the area's farmers. In addition,
investments in rural roads would have a positive effect on other economic
activities both within and outside of the project area. Social investments,
such as water supply systems and education, would improve living conditions
not only for project beneficiaries, but for virtually all residents of the
involved municipalities.
Sensitivity Analysis
9.05      Given the nature of the project and consequent uncertainties,
particular attention was paid to the calculation of switching values for
the most important parameters associated with the benefit and cost streams.
The results of this sensitivity analysis indicate that expected production
increases in rainfed rice, the project's principal crop, could be cut almost
in half before the project's estimated rate of return fell below an estimated
12% opportunity cost of capital. The fisheries component displays an even
lower degree of sensitivity to substantial decreases in expected fish
production. For the project as a whole, benefits would have to decrease by
25% to 30% or costs would have to increase by 35% to 40% before the overall
rate of return fell below a 12% opportunity cost of capital. A one-year delay
in all project benefits would cause the estimated rate of return to fall to
about 16%. Coupled with this delay, incremental benefits would have to
decrease by about 15% to 20% before the economic rate of return fell below the
opportunity cost of capital (Annex 5).
Employment
9.06      Project-induced changes in tenure, technology, and production
practices are expected to have a significant impact on employment. As is the
case in most other Northeast states, there is a substantial flow of both
temporary and permanent migration from rural to urban areas located within the
state, in neighboring states, and in the south of Brazil. Low on-farm labor
requirements because of land scarcity and insecurity of tenure for small
farmers have undoubtedly been a contributing factor to the migration flow.
Proposed project land and agricultural development activities would substan-
tially increase on-farm employment opportunities by about 20,650 worker-years
in Year Ten (full development). About 68% of these additional labor requirements



- 40 -
would be met by family labor. Similarly, project fisheries activities would
generate about 840 worker-years of additional employment for fishermen.
Ancillary activities, particularly production processing and marketing and
ship-building, repair and maintenance, would also provide additional permanent
employment opportunities.
Fiscal Impact
9.07      The costs of project investment per benefiting family would vary
according to the type of beneficiary. Costs for land distribution benefi-
ciaries would amount to about US$5,800 per family for the 3,500 families
without irrigation and about US$11,900 for the 1,500 families with irrigation.
Costs for the 1,500 current small owners who would benefit from project
demarcation and titling services would be about US$4,400 per family. Costs
for the 1,500 landless farmers not directly involved in project land activities
would be considerably less, about US$2,900. Finally, cost per existing small
owner with secure land tenure (1,500 total) and fishing family (1,800 total)
benefited would amount to about US$3,600 (Annex 3, Table 13).
9.08      Existing Government policies do not call for low-income families
or fishermen to pay for the costs of production support services or rural
infrastructure, although municipalities and villages often do contribute
to the maintenance of such facilities. The costs of roads would be recovered
indirectly through various taxes and license fees on road users. Many of the
production costs associated with the project would be partially recovered
through the credit system. The extent to which these costs would be recovered
in real terms is difficult to estimate because of the current inflationary
environment in Brazil and the Government s policy of providing heavily sub-
sidized rural credit. Assuming continuing inflation, real recovery of on-farm
and fisheries costs related to the project could well be low. Higher real
cost recovery is expected for the project's land and irrigation activities.
In line with federal policies, cost recovery for project irrigation
construction would average about 30% to 35%, which is acceptable in light of
the current and expected income levels of beneficiaries (Annex 2). The
proportion of land-related costs which would be recovered would depend on
the amount of public lands sold to small farmers and the amount of
incremental revenues derived from land taxation (Annex 1).
9.09      Following the project disbursement period, project activities
would give rise to additional recurrent costs to the Government of about
US$2.6 million per annum. These expenses would be incurred principally
for extension, but would also include some residual project administration,
road maintenance, education, and water supply costs. The additional
expenditures are expected, however, to be totally offset by additional
revenues of roughly US$4.0 million annually generated by the ICM (Tax on
Product Circulation) and FUNRURAL (the Assistance Fund for Rural Workers)
taxes which would be applied to most incremental project production, and by
additional revenues from the ITR (Land Tax).



- 41 -
Environmental Impact
9.10      The project would have a beneficial, although limited, environmental
impact. While the area under crop cultivation would roughly double, better
soil and water management practices would be emphasized, and soil depletion
and erosion are expected to decline significantly, particularly in the case of
land beneficiaries. In fisheries, the relatively small scale of project
activities is not expected to alter the existing ecological balance. Fishing
activities were explicitly formulated so as to maintain an equilibrium between
the size and quality of the fishing fleet and the known existing marine
resources, thereby protecting the self-perpetuation of these resources.
Nutritional Impact
9.11      Important nutritional benefits would mainly result from increases in
basic food production, but would also occur as a result of the promotion of
home gardens and the provision of nutrition education by the social extension
service. Project fishing activities would increase the availability within
the project area of a relatively low-cost, high-quality source of protein. In
addition, the improvement of marketing services, and particularly the extension
of COBAL-s SOMAR distribution system to the project area, would increase the
availability to rural consumers of basic food staples at reasonable prices and
help ensure a more equitable intra-regional food distribution.
Impact on Women
9.12      The proposed project has the objective of improving rural family
income, and the entire family is considered as the basic productive unit.
In addition to providing additional on-farm employment opportunities for
women, the project, mainly through social extension activities, would emphasize
the income-generating potential of, and provide assistance for, activities
commonly undertaken by women, such as fish processing and the production of
sweets. The project-s social infrastructure investments in education and
water supply would particularly contribute to improvements in the quality of
life for women. A significant number of women were actively involved in the
preparation of the project, and during implementation, attention would be
given to ensuring the continued participation of women in the Project Manage-
ment Unit, on the staff of the executing agencies, and most importantly, at
the local level, as project beneficiaries.
Project Risks
9.13      Various risks are associated with the proposed project. Given the
nature of project land activities which could produce important structural
changes within the project area, social, political, and technical difficulties
may arise. Potential problems and possible solutions were identified and
carefully analysed during project preparation and appraisal. On the technical



- 42 -
side, provisions have been made to ensure the availability of expert advice
and adequate training for project staff. The strong support demonstrated for
the project at the federal, state, and local levels of government would
contribute to the effective handling of difficulties should they arise. In
addition, beneficiary participation in project planning would considerably
lessen potential impediments to project success. Other risks are associated
with the need for close inter-institutional coordination. These risks would
be reduced by the establishment of a special project management unit and the
designation of a project coordinator within each executing agency; the
regular scheduling of inter-institutional project meetings at the state,
regional, and local levels; the implementation of a comprehensive and continuous
monitoring system of key indicators; ongoing evaluation; and detailed annual
planning based on local participation and information received from the
monitoring and evaluation efforts. All of these elements to strengthen
effective inter-institutional coordination would also allow for project
flexibility and the continuous refinement of the technical recommendations
proposed for agricultural and fisheries development, thereby reducing the
risks associated with the adaptation and dissemination of improved technology
and managerial practices. The uncertain availability of public and private
lands for project use and the emergence of land speculation also represent
potential project risks.  Initial results of the land acquisition program have
been encouraging, because of both the number of properties offered for sale
and the lack of significant price increases as a result. It is expected that
the land redistribution program could continue with public lands should land
prices rise too sharply. The monitoring system would keep project management
well informed about land-related developments in the project area (Annex 1).
Finally, an institutional problem which may contribute to delays in project
implementation is the present situation of the POLONORDESTE program through
which project funds would be channeled. In the past, this program has suffered
from a number of funding delays and administrative weaknesses, particularly at
the regional and federal levels. Although key officials have committed
themselves to improving the program's management, the speed and thoroughness
with which necessary measures are adopted will affect the quality and rate of
implementation of all POLONORDESTE-financed projects, including the proposed
project. Changes in POLONORDESTE management and procedures would be closely
monitored throughout project implementation.
9.14      Overall, the risks associated with the proposed project are accept-
able in view of the expected benefits which would accrue to the low-income
rural residents of the project area. In addition, the important institution-
building aspects of the project and its comprehensive focus on activities
related to land tenure improvements could positively influence future rural
development efforts in Piaui and throughout Northeast Brazil.



- 43 -
X. AGREENIENTS REACHED DURING NEGOTIATIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
10.01     During negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Federal
Government that:
(a) all necessary steps would be taken to ensure the availability
of adequate fisheries and agricultural credit, as defined
during the annual planning process, for project participants
(para 4.41);
(b) adequate counterpart funding would be available for the
effective and timely execution of the project, and project
facilities and services would be adequately operated and
maintained (para 5.02);
(c) the Bank would be given a reasonable opportunity to comment
on any substantial modifications in the project's annual
operating plans after their review by POLONORDESTE management
(para 6.04);
(d) quarterly monitoring reports would be sent to the Bank promptly
upon their completion (para 6.05), and copies of the audited
statements of project accounts, including references to the
adequacy of procedures used, would be provided to the Bank by
October 1 of each year (para 6.08);
10.02     During negotiations, assurances were obtained from the State
of Piaui that:
(a) it would take all necessary steps to ensure that land
acquisition targets were met (para 4.13); by October 1, 1981,
a complete program for land discrimination would be presented
to the Bank for approval; and by April 1, 1982, legal
assistance for small holders involved in discrimination would
be contracted under terms and conditions satisfactory to the
Bank (para 4.16);
(b) the detailed outline of the marketing study would be sent
to the Bank for review prior to the initiation of fieldwork
and the final version of the study would be presented to
the Bank by April 1, 1982; the marketing consultants' final
reports and other written material would be sent to the Bank
by October 1, 1981 for review; COBAL's first year feasibility



- 44 -
study and detailed plans for rice collection and marketing and
the SOMAR supply network would be sent to the Bank by
October 1, 1981 for review and approval; and installation and
operating plans for input stores and the input distribution
network would be sent to the Bank prior to installation
(para 4.27);
(c) a full-time EMATER-PI technician would be maintained in each
of the cooperatives participating in the project (para 4.27);
(d) feasibility studies for small-scale irrigation schemes would
be sent to the Bank for approval prior to completion of
detailed designs and construction; the State Water Commission
would be legally established by October 1, 1981; a water users
association would be set up prior to the construction of each
scheme; before the completion of the works in each of the
irrigation schemes, the State Water Commission would establish
a water charge scheme, satisfactory to the Bank, providing for
the full recovery of operating and maintenance costs and the
partial recovery of investment costs (para 4.30);
(e) annual work programs for the road 'component, including lists of
roads to be upgraded or constructed during each subsequent year,
would be presented to the Bank for review and comments by
October 1 of each year (para 4.35);
(f) CODERPI would prepare engineering designs and bidding documents,
award construction contracts, and supervise construction, all
in accordance with POLONORDESTE standards; and provide to the
