NORTHEAST BRAZIL
PIAUI RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
THE LAND COMPONENT
I. BACKGROUND DATA
1.        This annex supplements the information given in Chapter III of the
report on the state of Piaui and on the project area.   It includes additional
information on farm distribution and the land tenure situation, the land
market, land titling, registration, taxation, and institutional aspects,
focusing particularly on the recent development of state institutions as
a consequence of project preparation.
A. Land Tenure in the State of Piaui and the Project Area
2.        The state of Piaui features one of the highest concentrations of
land ownership in the Northeast, being surpassed only by the neighboring
state of Maranhao. Some 65% of the state's total population of about 1.5
million is rural, and 88% of all rural producers are landless. With an
area of approximately 25 million ha, only 10.3 million ha (41.2%) are offi-
cially owned by private parties (on the basis of INCRA cadastral records), the
balance being unregistered private holdings and public lands. Registered
private land is held by a total of 210,000 owners, among whom 151,000 (72%)
have less than 10 ha each and occupy only 3% of the privately held areas. At
the other extreme, 1,352 owners (less than 1%) have more than 1,000 ha each
and hold 44% of the area.
3.        The Vale do Parnaiba project area, with the highest population
density in the State, comprises 2.43 million hectares, of which about 1.7
million ha (70%) are suitable for agriculture. Only about one tenth of
the cultivable land is actually cropped, with the remaining 90% under forest,
bush fallow and extensive pasture (Annex 2, Table 6). The land tenure
situation is fairly homogeneous throughout the project area, although there is
a somewhat higher concentration of large estates in the Delta do Parnaiba
region. The majority of farmers in the project area (about 78%) are landless,
mainly renters. Among the 12,500 owners, some 30% with holdings above 100 ha
control 82% of the land, while 50% with holdings under 50 ha control only
about 8%.
4.        The majority of landless farmers in the project area are still
able to find rental lands, since livestock expansion, which has proved to be
a severe limiting factor elsewhere in the Northeast, is still modest (although
noticeable). Rental contracts are generally verbal and made for only one
crop cycle. The amount of leased land averages about 3 ha per family and
seldom exceeds 4 to 5 ha. Tenants usually clear and crop a different area



ANNEX 1
- 48 -                            Page 2
each year.  This system implies high labor use for both land clearing and
fencing in order to keep out the owner's freely roaming large and small
livestock . Rental fees are high (US$25 to US$50/ha annually), compared
with the average market price of land (about US$35/ha) and with the gross
value of average annual production/ha (about US$200). 1/ Rent must be paid in
kind (usually rice and/or babacu nuts). In fact, rent is often increased
because owners require a partial payment prior to the planting season,
in which case the renter must purchase rice at the highest annual price
level to make his payment. In addition to rental payments, obligatory uncom-
pensated services to the landlord persist under the traditional "sujeicao"
system still prevalent on some of the larger farms. Finally, land owners tend
to control the marketing channels in the project area for both rice and
babacu.
5.        Landlessness locks the poorest farmers into a system of small-scale,
low technology, annually shifting cultivation. Investment in modern inputs
or upgraded technology is practically impossible, and should profits accrue,
these are frequently offset by corresponding increases in rental obliga-
tions. While the landless are thus faced with very adverse arrangements,
paternalistic "benefits", traditionally associated with latifundia systems,
are being phased out. For example, only a small-minority of the landless live
on the farms they work and, due to the high incidence of absentee landlords,
support to families of landless workers in cases of illness or other emergencies
has become unreliable.
6.        With regard to the 8,800 owner-operators with farms under 100 ha
(representing 70% of project area landowners and accounting for 18% of privately
held land), declining soil fertility is a frequent problem, resulting from
overutilization of land as increasing population pressure has led to a reduction
of fallow periods (traditionally up to 8 years). Smallholders share some of
the problems of the landless, since they are partially dependent on rental
lands. In addition, insecurity of tenure due to inadequate documentation is a
serious problem throughout the project area.
B. The Land Market
7.        The land market in Piaui and in the project area is mainly limited
to medium and large landlords and a few private companies with little parti-
cipation by smallholders. Smallholders and especially landless farmers have
limited access to the land market because of the generally large size of
properties for sale combined with the limited availability of suitable land-
purchase credit. The land market is characterized by a low level of transactions
(annually less than 1% of farmland in the project area) and a particularly
low average land price (about US$35/ha). Over the past five to ten years, the
rate of increase in land prices has consistently been below the prevailing
rate of inflation, resulting in a decline in the real price of land. Land prices
vary from US$20 for uncleared forest to about US$70 to US$80 for good pasture
land. These price variations are usually related to the quality of land clearing
and the potential ioTr cattle taisiig.  SoiA gia:iUt  axLi, particularly, potential
1/   Annual output/ha is affected by high fluctuations due to climatic
conditions, i.e. renting fees can exceed 50% of the total value of
output in drought-affected years.



- 49 -                            ANNEX 1
Page 3
for water development, are not usually considered in price establishment.
For the majority of traditional families, land is valued principally as
a source of social prestige, although it is considered a good protection
against inflation. Land is, therefore, still not regarded generally as a
market commodity. This traditional concept might change if an increased
demand for land by private agricultural companies were to occur. Such an
increase in demand should result in a corresponding increase in land prices.
C. Land Titling, Registration and Taxation
8.        In the project area and in the state of Piaui in general, only
about 70% of the landowners have proper title. In addition, title records
are out of date; INCRA's records do not reflect the current situation; and
there is no reliable cadastre. The absence of proper titles negatively
affects the smallholders who cannot invest in their farms because of insecure
land tenure combined with a lack of access to credit. 1/ Titling services, up
to February 1981 the responsibility of COMDEPI 2/, have not always been
technically or legally reliable. Titling costs have generally been too
expensive for smallholders (US$20/ha, i.e. about 60% of the average price of
land 3/). These services have just been transferred to INTERPI (paras 10-13).
Title registering and recording within the state Department of Justice
(cartorio) is cumbersome. Titles are filed in books without modern recording
and retrieving systems. As a result, many titles have not been updated for
years. Frequently, even when properties are properly titled, they are not
well documented in the files. Therefore, existing cadastral maps are generally
unreliable. Given this situation, which is prevalent throughout Northeast
Brazil, INCRA, the federal land agency, has periodically conducted a cadastral
census based on the declaration of farmers for land taxation purposes.
According to this census, only 55% of the total geographic area is actually
declared. This is due to the underestimate and/or underdeclaration of private
land by landlords for tax evasion purposes combined with public lands which
are either underdeclared or not declared at all by the present occupants.
9.        Up until 1980, land taxation revenues in the state of Piaui were
insignificant 4/ due to an inefficient land taxation system combined with the
lack of a reliable data base. A new taxation system, including a new federal
land tax law, has recently been established to correct the past situation and
to stimulate better land use. Under this new system, land tax is progressive,
the tax rate varying directly with the size of the property and inversely
with the degree of efficiency and extent of exploitation. However, the
maximum taxation is only 3.5% of the actual land value, which may be updated
1/ Farm investment credit requires proper land documentation.
2/ Companhia de Desenvolvimento do Piaui (footnote 1, page 7).
3/ Includes demarcation, titling, and registering.
4/ Total 1979 land tax revenue in the project area was about Cr$930,000
(US$31,000) for 2.4 million ha, i.e. about US$0.01/ha.



ANNEX 1
- 50 -                           Page 4
periodically. Enforcing this new land taxation system presupposes a reliable
cadastre which presently does not exist for the project area, the state of
Piaui, or for the majority of the states in the Northeast. For this reason,
INCRA, which has already initiated the progressive establishment of a better
cadastre through land "discrimination" 1/ programs in limited areas outside of
the Northeast, is taking steps to dramatically increase these programs.
Since 1970, 50 million ha have been discriminated in the Northern and Central
Western regions of Brazil. A technical and legal approach has been developed
and, on that basis, new programs for land discrimination have been prepared
for areas in the Northeast with possible support from international development
institutions. 2/ Past land discrimination efforts, however, have not always
been supported by small farmers. Land discrimination has been considered by
them as a system to deprive them of their rights on the land they own and/or
occupy, even though the system was theoretically designed to protect the
rights of all holders and to recover illegally occupied public lands for
further redistribution. 3/ There may be some foundation for the lack of small
farmer support due to insufficient and inappropriate diffusion and explanation
campaigns where land discrimination has been initiated, combined with a lack
of legal assistance specific to small holders. Such defects would hopefully
be corrected in future programs, particularly under the project (para 28).
D. Institutional Aspects
10.       Up until 1979, the state of Piaui was not well equipped institution-
ally to deal with land issues in a rational and comprehensive manner. Land
services were not only insufficient, but scattered in different institutions
without a clear definition of land policy at the state level. COMDEPI 4/, a
multi-purpose state development company, was in charge of topographical
surveys and land development in the southern and southwestern parts of the
state. Its land operations, however, were mainly commercial, targeted towards
the installment of private companies and without a particular concern for
smallholders. In addition, COMDEPI's land-related technical capacity was
quite limited and generally of low quality. Titling services also within
COMDEPI were not effective (para 8). The state branch of INCRA was mainly
involved in carrying out cadastral surveys and some limited colonization
programs with DNOCS. A state office of the Federal Patrimony (Patrimonio da
Uniao), located in Parnaiba, was in charge of maintaining records for about
60,000 ha of federal land in the three northern coastal municipalities of the
project area. 5/
1/ The detailed determination of current land claims, and the identification
and demarcation of lands not legally claimed which could be subsequently
redistributed. The concept includes both topographical survey and title
search.
2/ A land discrimination program is currently under discussion with the IDB,
and the World Bank has also been approached on this matter.
3/ See World Bank report on the land tenure situation in Maranhao by J. Tendler,
December 1980.
4/  Companhia de Desenvolvimento do Piaui is in charge of mining, prospecting
and development, industrial development, and other matters in relation
to land.
5/ Luis Correia, Parnaiba and Buriti Dos Lopes.



- 51 -                           ANNEX 1
Page 5
11.       Since the end of 1979, largely due to the preparation of the Vale do
Parnaiba project, the state government has progressively established new
institutions to cope more efficiently with land issues. A land unit (LU) has
been created within CEPRO, a foundation linked to the State Secreteriat of
Planning, to prepare and initiate a land acquisition and redistribution
program within the proposed project area. Simultaneously, a state land-
purchasing fund (FUNVALE) was also established within CEPRO and funded with
Cr$100 million from POLONORDESTE for FY 1980/81. In addition, a State Land
Commission, made up of representatives from INCRA, EMATER, FETAG 1/ and the
local banks was created to review land purchase recommendations made by the LU
before final decision by the Secretary of Planning. With these new institutions,
the land acquisition program has been launched as a start-up activity before
actual project implementation. About 40,000 ha have already been acquired, of
which about 33,000 ha were privately owned.
12.       In addition, and in part because of these developments, the state
Government has moved to rationalize its land related institutional framework
in order to implement a consistent state land policy and avoid conflicts
of competency and orientation between agencies such as CEPRO, COMDEPI and
the State Secretariat of Agriculture. Accordingly, a State Land Institute
(Instituto da Terra do Piaui-INTERPI) was legally established in December
1980, to regroup all land-related activities within the same agency.
13.       According to the new law, INTERPI is a legally autonomous state
agency (autarquia) under the jurisdiction of the State Secretariat of
Agriculture.  INTERPI's main objectives are to:
(a)  participate in the establishment of state land policy;
(b) carry out settlement and/or colonization projects,
thereby promoting land distribution to landless farmers;
(c) represent the state in all land-related operations;
(d) define the State Land Patrimony, administer it, and
redistribute parts of it;
(e) attract funds for conducting colonization and other
land-related programs;
(f) handle titling services;
(g) organize the state rural cadastre;
(h) establish contracts with all agencies (public, private, national
and/or foreign) in relation to the financing, implementation,
technical assistance, and administration of land-related
projects;
IJ The State Federation of Rural Workers



- 52 -                            ANNEX 1
Page 6
(i) promote land expropriation, when necessary for public and/or
social reasons, and advise INCRA on that matter; and
(j) review the situation of "occupants" of public lands, giving
them preference in the acquisition of up to 100 ha.
In addition, INTERPI's land-related activities are to be integrated with a
comprehensive rural development program aimed at: (a) improving land distribu-
tion in the state of Piaui; (b) reducing the number of landless families;
(c) expanding land under cultivation by promoting better technologies and
providing farmers with technical assistance; and (d) fostering conservation of
natural resources and reforestation in appropriate areas. INTERPI's board of
directors is chaired by the State Secretary of Agriculture and made up of
representatives from the State Secretariats of Planning and Industry and
Commerce, INCRA, FETAG and the President of INTERPI. Land-related programs
previously conducted by other state agencies have already been legally
transferred, and would be progressively incorporated into INTERPI as this new
institution increases its operational capabilities (para 33).
II. THE LAND COMPONENT UNDER THE PROJECT
A. Long Term Objectives and Strategy
14.       On land matters, a long term development strategy for the state
of Piaui would aim to:
(a) offer small-scale farmers and rural workers increased access
to land in order to improve incomes and standards of living.
For the landless, this would mean the opportunity to purchase
land with appropriate repayment schemes, or to rent it under
fair contracts guaranteeing a rental period long enough to
allow profitable farm operations. For small owner operators
with farms too small to make a decent living, this would mean
the opportunity to increase farm size by purchasing or renting
complementary land;
(b) promote better land use in areas already cleared and under
crops and to incorporate new agricultural lands by establishing
rational colonization schemes accessible to landless farmers.
This would be carried out through the improvement of colonization
programs in the southern part of the state of Piaui and through
the systematic enforcement of land taxation. This would imply
the progressive establishment of a reliable cadastre with
complete and up-to-date ownership registration and titling
coupled with systematic soil surveys to determine agricultural
potential in relation to rainfall data and water resources; and
(c) simultaneously develop a corps of land specialists to manage
land-related programs at the state level.



- 53 -
ANNEX 1
Page 7
B. Objectives
15.       As the first step in a long-term strategy, the land component under
the project would aim to establish the operational basis for constructing a
sound land policy in the state of Piaui. This would be accomplished through
the implementation of a significant and comprehensive program of land activi-
ties in the project area over a five year investment period. In particular,
the land component would attempt to:
(a) develop low-cost, but technically sound, participatory
approaches for a range of land activities, including land
acquisition and redistribution, land discrimination, demarca-
tion, titling, and improvement of renting contracts; and
(b) build institutional capacity at the state level by providing
the newly created state land institute (INTERPI) with technical
assistance, staff training, equipment and adequate financing.
The five year program of land activities in the project area would primarily
include:
(a) the acquisition and redistribution of about 200,000 ha
to some 5,000 currently landless families; and
(b) the discrimination of about one third of the land in the
project area (about 900,000 ha) in order to progressively
establish a reliable cadastre with up-to-date and complete
ownership titling and registration; such a cadastre would
provide the basis for enforcing land taxation and for identifying
additional public land (Terras Devolutas) suitable for redistribu-
tion to small farmers.
The program would also include a set of actions to secure long-term tenure
rights over communal land for about 2,500 families in 8 coastal fishing
communities.
C. Strategy
16.       The strategy for implementing this program of land-related activities
would differ from that of past efforts involving schematic, costly, and
problem-ridden colonization projects. It would be based on the following
set of principles:
(a) a low-cost approach for land acquisition, including: (i)
the combination of various means of public and private land
acquisition (land purchasing on the market, expropriation,
donation from the federal and communal patrimony, and identi-
fication of public lands through land discrimination); (ii) the
concentration of land acquisition in areas where other properties
have been acquired; and (iii) the selection of good valley land
with Irrigation potential given the current lack of price
differential with other types of less productive land;



54                             ANNEX 1
Page 8
(b) flexibility in property allotment according to conditions
specific to each property acquired and beneficiary desires.
Beneficiaries would play a key role in the planning of their
allotment scheme and in the decision on final forms of tenure
(individual, communal, or some combination of both);
(c) a flexible system for land repayment adapted to existing
practices and conditions of beneficiaries;
(d) a minimization of population relocation. Preference would be
given to: (i) landless farmers already living on the properties
to be acquired; (ii) landless farmers cultivating parts of these
properties under rental arrangements; and (iii) other landless
farmers, and possibly small owner-operators, in the vicinity of
the properties;
(e) careful annual planning in relation to land actually acquired
for subsequent redistribution; social and economic infrastructure,
as well as the delivery of services, would be planned in support
of the properties acquired, focusing project activities on the
specific areas of land redistribution;
(f) continuous monitoring and evaluation directly associated
with annual planning;
(g) major support for the establishment and initial operations
of a State Land Institute (INTERPI), which would not only
carry out all land activities in the project area, but would
progressively implement land policy throughout the State of
Piaui; and
(h)  a strong linkage between land redistribution and agricul-
tural development with strong support from technical and support
services (applied research, extension, credit, marketing, seed
production, and input supply).
D. Land Activities
Description of Land Activities
Land Acquisition
17.       It is estimated that about 200,000 ha would be acquired and redis-
tributed to some 5,000 currently landless families over the 5-year project
investment period. This target has been defined on the basis of the progres-
sive establishment and strengthening of INTERPI's institutional absorption
capacity and the potential for purchasing private land on the market.
Estimates would be revised during the successive annual planning exercises.
It is ssible that the amount of land acquired and the number of benefi-
ciaries coula 'be 'nIgY\et &L'e to thae-  ieftton of mew   alc. and Prvate lands
for redistribution through the land discrimination process (para 26).



55 -                            ANNEX 1
Page 9
18.       The land acquisition program would be concentrated in the first
three years of the 5-year project investment period to allow for the subse-
quent allotment process and initial farm investment. The program has already
been initiated as a start-up activity, with about 50,000 ha already acquired.
It is expected that about 10,000 additional ha would have been identified for
possible acquisition prior to actual project implementation. The subsequent
acquisition rhythm has been estimated at an annual rate of about 50,000 ha for
the first three years of project implementation.
19.       The land to be acquired would originate from both private and
public sources and would be included in the public land available for
distribution by INTERPI. Private land would be purchased on the market
through FUNVALE, which would be annually replenished with federal POLONORDESTE
resources according to annual purchasing plans and actual repayment by land
beneficiaries. Private land could also be acquired through expropriation with
landowner compensation from FUNVALE. Expropriation, however, would remain
exceptional since it is politically difficult to implement and could create
considerable social tension. In addition, expropriation involves long and
cumbersome procedures with special intervention from INCRA headquarters and
authorization by the Federal Government. Public land would include municipal,
and possibly, federal public land (Patrimonio da Uniao), as well as newly
classified public lands (terras devolutas), which would become available as a
result of land discrimination. As far as possible, land purchasing would be
maintained at a constant and regular pace in order to avoid sudden fluctuations
in the land market; in the care of such unexpected fluctuations, land purchasing
would be temporarily slowed down or stopped while land redistribution would
continue on the basis of acquired public lands.
20.       It is presently difficult to estimate the amount of public land
that INTERPI could acquire and redistribute during the 5-year investment
period because of the lack of reliable data and experience in land discrimina-
tion in the state of Piaui. Accordingly, FUNVALE has been budgeted under the
assumption that the 200,000 ha would be private land either purchased on the
market or compensated in the case of expropriation. Public land would,
therefore, constitute a margin of security in case of difficulties in the
land market and/or in case of a higher beneficiary land requirement, which
could exceed 40 ha in some parts of the project area.
21.       The potential risks of the land acquisition program could be the
absence or the limited amount of private land for sale to the project, the
limited amount of public land actually available to INTERPI, and the emergence
of land speculation. With regard to the first risk, initial experiences with
land purchasing as a start-up activity before project implementation indicate
that the amount of land for sale is not only higher than expected (para 18),
but that the number of offers to the project is increasing. With regard to
the second risk, the state of Piaui is considered to have an abundance of
municipal public land. Initial experience in this respect is also encouraging,



- 56 -                             ANNEX 1
Page 10
with about 7,000 ha already donated for redistribution by two municipalities.
Since infrastructure such as water supply and feeder roads financed by the
project would be linked to land acquired in each municipality, such an
incentive is expected to stimulate offers of public land to INTERPI by the
municipalities. Careful selection would have to be made, however, given the
usual low to medium soil quality of public lands. Finally, with regard to
potential land speculation, the land market has thus far not shown excessive
increases in land price despite the initiation of the land acquisition program.
Accordingly, the annual replenishments for FUNVALE have been estimated on the
basis of a constant average price of land in real terms (US$35/ha, September
1980 price). Given this relatively low price compared to other areas in the
Northeast, slight increases could occur during the project life. These
increases, however, would probably be offset by the acquisition of free public
lands. In any case, the land market would be closely monitored over the
project disbursement period.
22.       The land acquisition program would be implemented by INTERPI,
through its acquisition unit (AU), with technical assistance from INCRA. AU
has already acquired experience during the 1980/81 land acquisition campaign
and has developed a suitable approach. This approach involves a sequence
of informative, administrative, legal and technical activities which can
be condensed into three main steps:
(a) Information, Promotion and Land Identification. Information
and promotional campaigns are held through meetings at the
municipal level with prefects and landlords, and through mass
communication. Such campaigns identify both private land for
sale and public land for donation.
(b) Land Evaluation. In the case of each estate for sale, this
step is initiated once an official offer (promessa de compra e
venda) is made by the landlord and transmitted to INTERPI.
Subsequently, a team from AU composed of an agriculturalist/soil
specialist, a sociologist and a lawyer conducts a field survey
and prepares the evaluation dossier. The agriculturalist/soil
specialist assesses land quality, agricultural potential,
and the existing infrastructure and farm investments. The
sociologist registers the landless families living on the
estate, analyzes the current renting and/or sharecropping
contracts, reviews the situation of close-by rural communities,
and identifies potential land beneficiaries. The lawyer
reviews the property records, including checks on the land
title, the tax situation, and any potential conflicts between
the property and neighboring estates. The team then establishes
the evaluation dossier, which is first reviewed by INCRA and then
presented to INTERPI management for final decision;
(c)  Land Purchasing.  Land to be purchased is selected according to
a set of criteria including the size of the estate, the percentage
of land suitable for agriculture, the quality of infrastructure,
the number of potential beneficiaries, and the proximity of
other estates already purchased. Once a positive decision is



57                             ANNEX 1
Page 11
reached, a topographical survey is conducted by a specialized
firm, in order to obtain reliable data on the exact size of the
estate and its topographical situation.  Simultaneously,
discussions between INTERPI management and the seller take
place to reach a price agreement on the basis of the initial
offer, the evaluation dossier, and the findings of the
topographical survey. Finally, the seller is paid with funds
from FUNVALE.
It is estimated that for each individual property to be purchased by INTERPI,
the whole process would take a maximum of three months. The time required
for each elementary operation has been estimated, and a critical path analysis
has been carried out on that basis. In the case of public land acquisition,
the elementary operations of the administrative process have also been identified.
The time required to carry out these has also been assessed and would be in
the same range as that for acquisition of private land.
Transitory Management
23.       As soon as an estate was acquired under the project, it would
come under a system of transitory management by a special unit of INTERPI, in
order to allow a participatory and technically sound redistribution process to
take place. INTERPI would establish a legal relationship with renters and/or
sharecroppers already farming on the purchased estate by confirming their new
status as land beneficiaries through a standard one-year contract whereby:
(i) beneficiaries would be authorized to continue cropping according to
previously practiced patterns; (ii) they would be able to obtain credit
through possession of required documents (carta de anuencia); and (iii) after
harvest, they would pay in rice to INTERPI a quantity equivalent to current
renting fees prevalent in the area, but which would be counted as an advance
payment for the lot each family would receive as a result of land
redistribution.
24.       It is estimated that the transitory management period would last
approximately one year, including only one agricultural campaign. During
this period, INTERPI would be assisted by EMATER field extensionists who would
be appointed to the new properties shortly after their purchase. A ratio of
about 200/250 potential beneficiaries per agent has been estimated for this
initial phase. 1/ Each agent would be in charge of conducting initial infor-
mation meetings to present the project to the beneficiaries and to explain
the system of transitory management and how beneficiaries are expected to
participate in the different steps of the redistribution process. Exten-
sionists would also assist INTERPI in carrying out complementary surveys to
prepare the allotment plan and in conducting meetings to present and discuss
proposed allotment schemes with beneficiaries (para 25). At the same time,
1/   This ratio of 1 extensionist for 200/250 beneficiaries would become 1 for
125 beneficiaries just after land distribution because of intensive tech-
nical assistance during the installment period. Some of the extension
staff appointed for transitory management would become local extension
managers because of their acquired experience. (See working paper no. 7
on rural extension, available in project file.)



- 58 -                           ANNEX 1
Page 12
technical training of beneficiaries would be initiated through a demon-
stration plot on the estate. Finally, extension agents would participate in
organizing the collection of the first land repayment in kind which is the
start-up activity for a crop purchase system. I/
Allotment Planning and Land Redistribution
25.       The allotment planning and land redistribution process would be
initiated just after land acquisition and carried out during the transitory
management period. This process would include four main phases:
(a) The preparatory phase would consist of the gathering of comple-
mentary data on which allotment would be based. On the
technical side, this phase would be initiated by the estab-
lishment of a topographical plan obtained by stereo-plotting
and photo-interpretation analysis of existing aerial photos 2/.
The main groups of soils and a first zoning for future allotment
would be defined. This first assessment made on the drawing
board would be refined by a deeper soil reconnaisance survey to
be conducted in the field according to USBR recommendations. 3/
Simultaneously, present land use would be reviewed with a
detailed evaluation of existing farm investments. Current and
potential water resources would also be carefully assessed. On
the sociological side, complementary surveys of potential
beneficiary families would be carried out. Particularly
careful attention would be paid to the selection of beneficiaries
who have not either rented or sharecropped a piece of land on
the property to be distributed. This selection would attempt
to minimize population movements and to avoid any disruptions
in close-by rural communities.
(b) The allotment phase would begin right after the preparatory
phase. It would involve participation by beneficiaries in the
planning of their future lots through an iterative process
between the drawing board at INTERPI headquarters and meetings
with beneficiaries in the field. A first allotment scheme
would be established on the basis of the data gathered during
phase I; this scheme would then be presented to, and discussed
with, the beneficiaries. This first scheme would then be
modified in accordance with the beneficiaries' recommendations
and once again presented for further discussions. It has been
assumed that, on average, the final allotment scheme for a
particular estate would be agreed upon after two runs.
1/ See working paper no. 9 on marketing available in project files.
2/ Aerial photos with 1/25,000 scale exist for the entire project area.
3/ Soil observation and sampling every 250 m; incteased saapling in case of
high soil heterogeneity.



- 59 -                            ANNEX 1
Page 13
(c)  The distribution phase would be carried out on the basis
of the final agreed allotment scheme. This phase would
include the actual demarcation of lots in the field, the
building of initial necessary infrastructure such as water
supply and feeder roads, and land titling. Land demarcation
would be carried out by specialized firms selected by national
competitive bidding. Infrastructure would be constructed
partly by state institutions and partly by specialized private
firms selected through state and national competitive bidding
procedures. Land titling would be carried out by INTERPI
with the assistance of INCRA. Each land beneficiary would be
established on the respective lot and would be committed
through a written contract to follow a set of specifications
for the development of a rational farming system (e.g. minimum
annual rhythm of land clearing, strip cropping on contour
lines, prohibition of subletting and sharecropping, selected
seed use) and to repay the land according to terms agreed upon.
Land titles would be withheld by INTERPI during the land
repayment period and then released to beneficiaries.
Allotment planning would be carried out by specialized teams, comprised of
photointerpreters/soil specialists, agriculturalists/allotment specialists,
civil engineers, water development specialists, sociologists, and lawyers, who
would be assisted by support staff, including topographers and designers. For
the three above-mentioned phases, the time required for each elementary
operation has been estimated as well as the amount of specialized staff
required. A critical path analysis has been carried out, as well as an
estimate of overall INTERPI operational staffing needs (para 38).
(d)  The land repayment plan.  Because financial resources
for land purchase credit are non-existent at present, a simple
Land Repayment Plan has been conceived in keeping with the
financial situation and the expected farm investment and
repayment capabilities of potential beneficiaries. The plan
would ensure about 25% to 30% recovery of average real land
purchase price and the costs of demarcation and titling. Under
this plan, beneficiaries would pay for their land in rice in
four installments. The total amount of rice to be paid would
be equivalent to the value of land assessed during the allotment
phase, taking into account soil quality and production potential,
existing infrastructure (i.e. irrigation, water supply, storage
and farm buildings) and accessibilty. Each annual installment,
to be collected in the fields by the cooperatives for INTERPI,
would be equal to or less than the amount of rent currently paid
by area tenants, i.e. about 235 kg of paddy rice per cultivated



-60-                              ANNEX 1
Page 14
hectare. As a first estimate, a 35 ha to 40 ha lot in dry
farming would cost about Cr$120,000 (US$2,150), including
acquisition, demarcation, and titling. A 25% repayment in real
terms would be equivalent to about 2 tons of paddy rice to be
repaid in 5 years (including the transitory period). In case
of unfavorable climatic conditions, debts would be easily
rescheduled in paddy rice terms, since the price of this
commodity has been almost indexed to inflation. Similarly, in
case of a good harvest, beneficiaries would have the possibility
of advancing their land repayment. Farmers who do not grow rice
would have the option of paying the equivalent amount in cash
instead of in paddy rice, the amount to be based on the current
market price at the local municipal level. During transitory
management, beneficiaries would pay rent in paddy rice to
INTERPI. The rice would be collected for INTERPI by area
cooperatives, processed, and subsequently transferred to COBAL,
which would pay INTERPI. INTERPI, in turn, would pay the
cooperatives special fees for rice collecting and processing.
In cases where no cooperative exists in the area, the rice
collection would be directly carried out by COBAL. Each
beneficiary would be credited in kind (number of kg of paddy
rice) for the rent paid. This credit would be deducted from
the total amount of rice to be paid once he receives his lot.
This Land Repayment Plan is already in effect for the properties
acquired during 1980/81. First reactions from beneficiaries are
favorable to the plan; they prefer a short repayment period in
kind rather than a long repayment in money as is the case with
traditional land purchase credit for which they are reluctant
to commit themselves, since they do not exactly measure the
impact of high inflation. For the Government, this formula is
also attractive since farmer would repay about 25% to 30% of
costs in real terms compared with less than 5% in the case of
land purchase credit.
Land Discrimination
26.       It is estimated that about one-third of the land in the project
area (about 900,000 ha) would be surveyed, demarcated, and properly titled
over the 5-year project investment period. Expected benefits from this land
discrimination program would be: (i) the identification of new public lands
(terras devolutas) for redistribution 1/; (ii) title review and regularization
for all owners and particularly for some 1,500 smallholders within the target
group who currently do not have proper titles and related tenure security.
This lack effectively bars them from obtaining farm investment credit; and
(iii) the establishment of a sound data base (complete cadastre and title
recording system) for enforcing the new land taxation system and for carrying
out further land action in the project area.
1/ "Generation" of new public lands through the land discrimination process
varies widely according to INCRA's first experiences and, therefore, is
particularly difficult to estimate ex ante. All suitable public lands
identified would be included in the land redistribution program.



- 61 -                            ANNEX 1
Page 15
27.       The land discrimination program would be concentrated during the
second, third and fourth years of the project investment period in order not
to overload INTERPI in its initial operations and simultaneously to allow
for subsequent demarcation, titling, and land redistribution in the case
of suitable newly identified public lands. The area to be discriminated is
currently being selected and would be fully identified during the first
year. Selection criteria include the ratio of INCRA-registered land to total
land in the area, the quality of soils and potential for water development,
the proximity to areas where the land redistribution program is taking place,
the existence of widespread land tenure uncertainty, and the number of
potential beneficiaries.
28.       The land discrimination program, including demarcation and titling,
would be carried out by INTERPI, with close cooperation and technical assistance
from INCRA. This program would involve a sequence of informative, administra-
tive, legal, and technical activities which are well described in the INCRA
operational manual (Sistematica de Discriminacao) (para 9). Under the project,
however, the implementation of land discrimination activities would be improved
through better information and a more effective protection of the rights of
smallholders. It would also be closely coordinated with the land acquisition
and redistribution program and with other land-related activities.
29.       Land discrimination activities would be grouped in five main
sequential phases:
(a) Phase I would be the initial survey of the area selected for
land discrimination. This survey would include a systematic
land title review in the files of the cadastre offices
(cartorio), complemented by field visits to check the actual
situation of each farmer and to transcribe this information on
a draft cadastral map established from aerial photos. Phase I
would be completed by the preparation of a report which would
be the technical and legal basis for further steps.
(b) Phase II would correspond to the actual discrimination between
private and public lands. This would be instituted with the
appointment by INCRA of a special commission, comprised of
a lawyer, an agriculturalist and an assistant who would be
responsible for land discrimination in a particular area.
Under the project, there would be three special commissions,
each working an area of about two municipalities. Staff
appointed to these commissions would receive special training,
including an overview of the entire project, its objectives and
the interrelationship between land discrimination and the other
land-related activities. The first action of the commission
would be to inform all the farmers about the discrimination
process and to request them to prepare their dossiers for
presentation to the commission. Mass diffusion, through
displays in official buildings, publication in newspapers,
and announcements on the radio, would be complemented by an



- 62 -                           ANNEX 1
Page 16
educational campaign through the EMATER network. The project
would also support special legal assistance to smallholders
in the form of three lawyers to help them prepare their documents.
These lawyers would work in liaison with the three commissions
of INTERPI, but would belong to a private non-profit organization
to be contracted at the beginning of Project Year 2. The actual
land discrimination would be carried out on the basis of the
Phase I report, the individual dossiers, and complementary
surveys, if requested. In the case of private land properly
titled and demarcated, ownership would be reconfirmed by the
commission. In other cases involving private lands (improper
titles, insufficient and/or erroneous demarcation, conflicting
claims between private parties), the situation would be regularized
according to the committee's decision. In the case of occupied
public land, the commission would propose adequate solutions to
the claimants (e.g. sale of the area under agriculture to
occupants, and devolution of any uncleared land to the state
patrimony) in line with Brazilian land law 1/. If claimants do
not accept commission proposals, the case would be turned over
to the judicial system.
(c) Phase III would correspond to the resulting land demarcation
and titling. Demarcation would be carried out by specialized
firms selected by national bidding. Titling would be carried
out by INTERPI, as one of its new functions. Costs related to
demarcation and titling would be fully paid by land owners,
with the exception of smallholders in the target group, who
would pay only 5% of these costs. The number of these
beneficiaries has been estimated at about 1,500, 50% of the
total number of smallholders in the areas selected for
discrimination.
(d) Phase IV would correspond to the redistribution of new public
lands identified through the land discrimination process; this
redistribution would be part of the land redistribution
program under the project.
(e) Phase V would complete the discrimination process by setting
up an improved cadastre with up-to-date cadastral maps and a
land data bank with a title-recording and retrieving system
within INTERPI. The project would assist in the financing of
related equipment and technical assistance for this purpose.

E. Organization and Management
30.       The overall program of land-related activities would be caried out
by INTERPI. The Land Unit (LU) as well as FUNVALE, currently within CEPRO,
are expected to be transferred to INTERPI in April/May 1981. The LU, a team of
10 professionals plus support staff, would be the initial core of INTERPI
staff; INTERPI will work almost exclusively for the project during initial
implementation. Later, INTERPI is expected to take on other land-related
projects and to become responsible eventually for implementing land policy in
the state of Piaui.
31.       With regard to the future organization of INTERPI, a temporary
working group has been appointed by the Secretariats of Agriculture and
Planning to prepare a document on the rules and regulations of the institution.
This working group, which includes a lawyer from INCRA, has already visited
similar institutions in other states of Brazil (Para, Ceara), and a first
draft of the document on rules and regulations has been presented. This
document is expected to be discussed and refined by both the State Government
and INCRA. The final draft would be presented to the Bank for comments prior
to negotiations.
32.       Since the organization and management of INTERPI has not been
finalized yet, staff requirements and related costs for the project have been
estimated on the basis of the system of organization proposed by the working
group for INTERPI. Under the proposed system, INTERPI would be organized into
four departments, three functional and one operational. The three functional
departments would include:
(a) a legal department (procuradoria juridica);
(b) a department for personnel, administration and finances; and
(c) a department of technical services, including mapping,
topography, photo-interpretation/soil surveying, cadastre,
titling services, and title recording.
33.       The operational department would be comprised of operational units
corresponding to on-going programs. In the case of the project, five units
would be created, one each for land acquisition, transitory management,
redistribution, discrimination, and the pilot scheme for rental contracts.
Each of these units would be fully responsible for the corresponding activity
and would receive support from the functional departments. In addition, this
department would have a monitoring and planning section.
34.       INCRA would play a key role in the implementation of the land
component, particularly in the early stages. As the leading federal agency in
land matters, INCRA would provide guidance, training and technical assistance
for the initial development of INTERPI. In addition, INCRA would directly
participate in operational matters, including: (i) land acquisition and



- 64 -                             ANNEX 1
Page 18
redistribution: INCRA would systematically review the evaluation of estates
before actual purchasing by INTERPI and would review allotment schemes prior
to land demarcation; and (ii) land discrimination: because of its experience,
INCRA would be responsible for training the INTERPI discrimination unit and
would second staff to INTERPI (one lawyer, three agriculturalists) for the
establishment of the three special commissions for land discrimination (para 28).
F. Staffing Requirements
35.       Staffing requirements for INTERPI have been estimated on the basis
of project requirements, although this institution would eventually require
additional staff as it takes on full responsibility for Piaui land-related
programs outside of the project area. Staff requirements for the project have
been calculated on the basis of a 50,000 ha annual rhythm for the land
acquisition and redistribution program, and a total of about 900,000 ha for
land discrimination. For each of these programs, the staff required has been
estimated on the basis of sequential tasks to be completed. The total number
of staff required has been checked through an aggregate critical path analysis.
These estimates were made on the assumption that work productivity would
progressively increase because of heavy training and experience gained during
the first years of implementation.
36.       By the third year of the project, INTERPI would require a total of
57 professionals plus support staff to carry out the land component; 23
professionals would be required for land discrimination alone. This total
includes seconded INCRA staff. INTERPI's professional staff would include
land-specialized lawyers, agriculturalists/land development specialists,
sociologists, photointerpreters/soil specialists, financial analysts, civil
engineers, accountant/procurement officers and communication experts. This
staff would be hired as needed by INTERPI, with the assistance of a consultant
during initial implementation.
37.       The project would exclusively finance the staff required for project
activities. The staff financed under the project would increase from Project
Year One (29 professionals) to Project Year Three (57 professionals); it would
then decrease to 12 profesionals in Project Year Five due to the concentration
of land acquisition and land discrimination during the first years of project
implementation. However, assuming satisfactory results, it is expected that
land acquisition and redistribution, as well as land discrimination, would
continue beyond years three and four in the project area. If these activities
were to continue at the same level after the project investment period, about
six additional years would be required for land discrimination to cover the
entire project area, and about 5 to 10 years would be needed for land
acquisition and redistribution. Such operations could be partially financed
through sales of public lands recovered by discrimination and through increased
land tax revenues.



- 65 -                             ANNEX 1
Page 19
G. Training and Technical Assistance
38.       Training programs and technical assistance would play a key role
in the development of project land-related activities and in the successful
establishment of INTERPI. The main objective would be to provide INTERPI
with both public and private expertise from INCRA headquarters in Brasilia,
state land institutions, and specialized consultants, with accumulated
experience in the Northeast as well as in Central and South Brazil. In
addition, INTERPI would not perform all the tasks related to land programs,
but rather would plan, organize, and control them.   It would rely heavily on
specialized private firms to carry out substantial parts of the projected
programs (e.g. technical surveys, demarcation, infrastructure).
39.       Training would be organized with the assistance of both INCRA
and specialized consulting firms, whose contracts with INTERPI would system-
atically include training responsibilities. Training programs would include:
(i) initial training before operations, including the presentation of the
project, its objectives and strategy, in relation to present conditions
and development constraints in the project area; (ii) on-the-job training
in the field to transfer practical expertise to new staff; and (iii) modular
training on specific technical matters, organized through short seminars.
Annual planning would also be considered a training exercise because it
includes the review of past operations and the definition and endorsement of
the new annual work program. Finally, visits to other land-related projects
in other states of Brazil would be organized.
40.       Total technical assistance has been estimated at 111 months of
consultants, excluding technical assistance provided by INCRA. Main areas
where technical assistance would be required are: (i) INTERPI's initial
establishment, including organization and management, recruitment and
training, finances, and planning (52 man-months); (ii) new technology on land
assessment, topography, aerial surveying and land development (33 man-months);
and (iii) new technology on title recording, cadastral mapping, and computing
activities (26 man-months). In legal matters, INCRA is expected to play a key
role in technical assistance. With regard to (i), four high-level consultants
would be contracted during initial implementation in the following areas:
(a) administration and finances; (b) personnel, recruitment, and training;
(c) organization of technical services; and (d) planning and operations.
These consultants would work full time during project year one to help INTERPI
management in its initial organization.
H. Liaison with the Other Project Components
41.       The entire project has been conceived around the land component
and particularly the land acquisition and redistribution program which would
constitute the basis for planning and organizing the implementation of the
other components (technical support and delivery services, social and physical
infrastructure). This implies a strong liaison between INTERPI, the project



- 66 -
ANNEX 1
Page 20
unit, and the other executing agencies. Special attention would be paid to
the liaison between INTERPI and (i) the planning and monitoring group within
the Project Unit, given the project's annual planning exercise; (ii) EMATER,
given its key role in land-related activiites; and (iii) the executing agencies
involved in physical infrastructure (roads, water supply, irrigation) and in
marketing, due to the land repayment scheme (para 25).
I. Costs
42.       The total project cost of the land component has been estimated
at US$16.8 million 1/ (Cr$938.2 million in September 1980 prices). This
would include the costs related to: (i) land purchasing through FUNVALE,
US$4.6 million (27%); (ii) land development through the acquisition and
redistribution program, US$5.9 million (35%); (iii) land discrimination,
including titling and demarcation, US$5.3 million (32%); (iv) pilot renting
scheme, US$0.25 million (2%); and (v) special technical assistance for INTERPI
management, US$0.66 million (4%).
