NORTHEAST BRAZIL
PIAUI RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT
I. BACKGROUND DATA
1.       This annex supplements the information given on the project area
in Chapter 3 of the report. It includes additional information on physical
features (climate, soils and water resources), the present agricultural
situation (land use, cropping patterns, farm typology, and production), and
institutional aspects (extension, applied research and seed production).
Finally, the main issues and constraints for agricultural development are
summarized.
A. Physical Features
2.        The project area, covering about 24,000 km2 (one tenth of the
state total), contains 26 municipalities stretching about 400 km along the
middle and lower Parnaiba River. This area is part of the Parnaiba sedimentary
basin, which is characterized by a low plateau (chapada, less than 100 meters
above sea level), dissected by the broad valleys of the Parnaiba River system.
This area has good agricultural potential, with soils of medium fertility,
particularly in the river valleys; good rainfall during December-June (1,000 mm
to 1,600 mm yearly); a lower risk of drought than in many areas of the
Northeast; and substantial surface and underground water resources. The
project area can be divided into three ecologically different subareas: the
Delta do Parnaiba subarea, located in the north, covering 44% of the total
project area with 10 municipalities and 66 km of coastline;the Teresina
subarea, in the center, covering 31% with 6 municipalities, including Teresina,
the state capital; and the Medio Parnaiba subarea, in the south covering 25%
with 10 municipalities (Maps 1 and 2).
Climate and Rainfall
3.        The project area is characterized by a six to seven month rainy
season from December/January to May/June with an average temperature of about
26oC and average relative humidity between 70% and 85%; and a five to six month
dry season over the rest of the year with average temperatures of 27oC to 3OoC
(highest temperatures in October) and an average relative humidity ranging
between 41% and 70% (lowest humidity in September). Annual rainfall
variation is low to medium, with variation coefficients ranging from 16% to
45%. Rainfall is highly concentrated, with 95% to 97% of annual rainfall
occurring during the rainy season and about 60% occurring during a three-month
peak period from January to March (Table 1; Maps 1 and 2).



ANNEX 2
Page 2
- 68 -
Soils
4.        According to the project area's topography, soils have been classi-
fied in three main categories:
(a) Soils on the mesa (chapada): These soils are shallow to mod-
erate in depth, light brown to yellow sandy Latosols. Nat-
ural fertility and productivity are low to medium, yet under
good management, the soils are capable of producing accept-
able yields; however, shallow stony areas unsuitable for
cultivation occur in some parts.
(b) Soils on the valley side slopes: These soils are usually
rocky and generally unsuitable for agriculture; where finer
soil has accumulated however, non-mechanized cropping is
possible if good protection is provided against erosion; in
fact, farmers prefer the slopes to the flat plateau lands for
cultivation because of a lower drought risk due to the
combination of run-off, underground seepage water and rainfall.
(c) Soils in the valleys: These soils are white to light
grayish sandy loams to loamy fine sands; vegetation includes
the babacu palm, which is considered an indicator of relatively
good cropland for rice and other crops. These are generally
the best soils of the project area, with a relatively higher
clay percentage and better moisture retention.
5.        Despite these differences in soil characteristics, the soils
of the project area are commonly affected by the following problems:
(a) fertility is generally low;
(b) moisture retention is low because of a low organic
matter content (1% to 3%), as well as a low clay content
(about 10%); as a result, readily available moisture is only
about 10% of volume in the majority of cases;
(c) infiltration is abnormally low (below 7 cm/day) due to
capping during heavy rains from January to March, particularly
where soil surface remains uncovered with vegetation; soil
permeability is satisfactory, however (50 cm/day); and
(d) erosion is usually high as a result of heavy rains
coupled with the lack of structure in the surface soil.
6.        Extensive soil surveys related to potential irrigation projects
along the Parnaiba River and its tributaries have been carried out by DNOCS
in some parts of the project area. These surveys have been complemented
by general soil reconnaissance through the "Radam" project 1/.  Due to
these efforts, a general soil map covering the overall project area has
been established. In addition, preliminary soil evaluation for about 40,000 ha
has been conducted as part of the land acquisition program (Annex 1). All of
these surveys show that:



ANNEX 2
- 69 -                               Page 3
(a) about 60% to 70% of the project area is suitable for agriculture;
(b) the Medio Parnaiba subarea is characterized by a relatively
high percentage of plateau land (about 60% to 70%) and slopes
(5% to 10%); soils are potentially more productive;
(c) in the Delta do Parnaiba subarea, lowlands and valleys account
for 60% to 70% of total area, whereas plateau land and related
slopes represent only 30% to 40%. Sandy soils with relatively
low productivity are frequent; valley lands, however, are
normally more fertile with a substantial accumulation of finer
soil elements (clay content may reach 20% to 25%); and
(d) the Teresina subarea represents a transition zone containing
about 50% mesaland and slopes and 50% lowlands and valleys.
7.        With regard to the land already acquired (Table 2), the percentage
of land suitable for agricultural production (18% with medium fertility soil
and 36% with low fertility) appears to be below average for the project area.
This is due to the inclusion of low-quality public land and the absence of
valley land in the first properties that have been acquired. Properties now
being purchased present a higher fertility level and comprise more valley land
since the program of land acquisition has been reoriented towards properties
along the rivers with substantial irrigation potential (Tables 2-3).
Water Resources
8.        Surface and underground water resources are abundant throughout the
project area and go far beyond the demand from existing or anticipated irriga-
tion schemes. With regard to surface water, the Parnaiba River drains most of
the state-s waters with an average flow of about 650 m3/s and an annual volume
of about 20 billion m3 2/. The Parnaiba River has three main tributaries
(Caninde, Poti and Longa Rivers), which cross the project area and offer
excellent opportunities for irrigation. Many other smaller permanent rivers
also flow directly or indirectly into the Parnaiba River. The Delta do
Parriaiba sub-area presents the state-s highest potential for irrigation
development at a relatively low cost. At the river town of Porto, the Parnaiba
valley gradually begins to broaden out into flood plains (varzeas) which
increase in size and importance toward the river mouth. These flood plains
consist of low-lying basins and levies, formed when tributary rivers and
streams join the Parnaiba. The lower parts of the varzeas are flooded annually.
Surface water flows, however, are characterized by large fluctuations (variation
coefficient 62%) between dry and rainy seasons; in the case of the Parnaiba
1/   General soil reconnaissance of the large Amazon region, using radar
technique. Such a soil reconnaissance, however, does not provide a
sufficient basis for project planning because of imprecise differentiation
of fertility levels, and the absence of detection of Ph and aluminum-
toxicity-related problems.
2/   Average flow measured at Porto fluvial station, Delta do Parnaiba subarea.



ANNEX 2
- 70 -                                Page 4
River, the base flow amounts to only 51% of the average.  In addition, there
are daily flow fluctuations due to the operation of the Boa Esperanca
hydroelectric power station upstream.
9.       Groundwater is present throughout the project area, with re-
sources estimated at billions of cubic meters. Accessibility is variable in
terms of well yields and depths, however. While there is no major limitation
governing water supply schemes for rural communities, there are limitations
for irrigation, which requires a minimum yield (about 7L/sec) and a maximum
depth (about 100 m) for wells to keep the investment cost economically
acceptable. As a rough estimate, about half of the project area has a potential
for irrigation with underground water; this potential is highest in the Delta
do Parnaiba subarea.
B. Existing Agricultural Situation
Land Tenure and Farm Distribution
10.       About 70% (about 1.7 million ha) of the project area is suitable
for agriculture. Of this cultivable land, only about 10% is actually culti-
vated, with the remaining 90% being comprised of a combination of forest, bush
fallow and extensive pasture land. There are some 57,700 farmers in the
project area. About 22% (12,500) of these farmers are owners and about
78% (45,200) are landless, mainly renters. Details on land tenure and farm
distribution are given in Annex 1 and Table 6.
Land Use, Cultivated Area and Yields
11.       Land use has been roughly estimated on the basis of state sta-
tistical data crosschecked with additional survey data from 179 farmers
and with the evaluation of large properties within the ongoing land acqui-
sition program. About 180,000 ha (approximately 10% of the land suitable
for agriculture), are cropped annually, with annual fluctuations mostly due to
climatic hazards, particularly during the planting period. About 88% of the
cultivated area is intercropped, with upland rice as a dominant crop,
accompanied by corn, beans, and manioc. Single-crop plantings, mostly rice,
account for about 12% of the cropped area. Average yields are very low (about
0.7 t/ha for rice, 0.3 t/ha for corn, 0.2 t/ha for beans and 4.0 t/ha for
manioc) and fluctuate according to annual climatic conditions and type of
soils.
12.       The proportion of land used in relation to fallow/uncleared areas
varies in relation to farm size: owner operators with less than 10 ha usually
crop about 80% of their property with no possibility of a long fallow rotation
system. This percentage of cropped land in relation to total farm land
regularly decreases as farm size increases (33% for farms between 10 ha and 25
ha, 14% for farms between 25 ha and 100 ha, and about 7% for farms over 1,000
ha).  There are several cases of large pxopeTtties whe're Croea land accouints
for 5% or less of the total cultivable area (Tables 4-5).



ANNEX 2
- 71 -                               Page 5
Cropping Patterns and Technology
13.       The project area is characterized by a great homogeneity in cropping
patterns, with limited variations according to farm size, land tenure status,
and ecological subarea. Rainfed subsistence farming, with manual cultivation
and low technology under a shifting cultivation system, is generally practiced.
The small differences in cropped area between farms with comparable cultivable
areas are mainly related to differences in the existing labor force. On the
basis of data obtained from the farm survey, a worker clears between 1 ha
and 1.5 ha of forest or bush fallow annually, utilizing the slash and burn
system. He then plants about 70% to 75% of the area with upland rice inter-
cropped with corn and manioc; rice intercropped with beans and cotton accounts
for another 10% to 15% of the area. Clearing and fencing of the area take him
more than a month per hectare each year. He usually plants late (January/
February) to take advantage of high soil moisture, and harvests in April or
May., Rice harvesting is performed with a knife, and takes about one worker-month
per hectare. Local medium-to-long-cycle varieties (120 to 150 days for rice)
are traditionally used, with no application of organic or chemical fertilizers
or pesticides.
14.       According to the survey, the differences between landless farmers
and owner-operators are the following:
(a) On average, a landless farmer has a 2.8 worker labor force
and clears and crops about 3.2 ha of land annually (the fallow
duration is on average 6 to 8 years). Clearing and cropping
occupy about 55% of his available labor force (360 worker-days).
There are two peak periods: clearing and fencing (July to
November) and harvesting (April/May). The remaining unutilized
working capacity (290 worker days) is usually sold outside the
farm (100 worker days on average) and/or employed for other
non-farm activities when these opportunities exist. Landless
farmers traditionally do not have cattle, but small livestock
(pigs and poultry) activities do take place. Vegetable gardening
is fairly rare.
(b) An owner-operator with up to 100 ha has a 3.7-worker labor
force on average, of which 1.2 is hired labor 1/; about
5.3 ha of land is cropped. Labor productivity is slightly
higher than that shown for landless farmers (1.43 ha per active
worker compared with 1.14 ha), although this is probably due to
the hiring of outside labor at peak periods. The fallow
duration depends on the total area of the farm and varies from
1 to 8 years. Cropping patterns and technology levels are
usually the same as those for landless farmers. The owner-
operator has some cattle (3 to 4 head) and also small livestock.
1/   1.2 man-years combining permanent and temporary labor.



- 72 -                                ANNEX 2
Page 6
15.       A few farmers are already applying improved technologies.  These
include methods of increasing labor productivity, such as the use of animal
traction (about 7% of the owner-operators), tractor-renting (about 12%), and
even the use of full mechanization in very rare cases. With animal traction,
labor productivity increases sharply from 1.4 ha per active worker to about 2
ha. This increase could be much higher, however, if better implements,
combined with technical assistance and adequate credit schemes, were available.
Tractors are usually rented for soil preparation and allow for early planting;
on average, labor productivity with tractor-renting reaches 3 to 4 ha per
active worker. Chemical fertilization is extremely limited, but when properly
used, has shown financially viable results.
16.       Despite substantial surface and underground water resources,
irrigation is still an exception in the project area. DNOCS is operating
a pilot irrigation project of some 500 ha (200 farmers), with a possible
expansion up to 2,000 ha in the municipality of Luzilandia. DNOCS has
also contracted a consulting firm to prepare an irrigation development
plan for about 16,000 ha along the Parnaiba and Longa Rivers; feasibility
studies for parts of this plan have already been completed. In addition
to these projects, a few farmers have their own small irrigation systems
which permit double cropping by pumping, and some farmers secure their
rainfed crop by utilizing complementary irrigation.
C.  Supporting Services
17.       Extension in the rural areas of Piaui is the responsibility of
EMATER-PI, which is an autonomous state company administratively under the
Secretary of Agriculture, but with strong links to the federal extension
agency, EMIBRATER, mainly for funding and technical supervision. EMATER
is concerned with both agricultural and social extension. The greater
emphasis thus far has been on agricultural extension oriented towards me-
dium and large farmers, although EMATER's recent involvement in implement-
ing new POLONORDESTE and INAN projects has served to partially reorient
extension towards small farmers. In addition, social extensionists, all
of whom are females, work with the families of small producers. In the
project area, 49 agricultural field extension agents (22 agriculturalists
and 27 agricultural technicians) and 17 social extensionists are distribu-
ted over 18 local extension offices. Of this total staff working in the
project area, 23 agricultural field extension agents (13 agriculturalists
and 10 agricultural technicians) and 9 social extensionists are currently
paid under the Delta do Parnaiba POLONORDESTE project, which will be ab-
sorbed by the proposed Piaui Rural Development Project - Vale do Parnaiba.
18.       Agricultural research in Piaui is the responsibility of EMBRAPA-PI,
a branch of the Federal Enterprise for Agricultural Research, which
has a central experimental station in Teresina. Until recently, relatively
little effective research has focussed on technical problems specific
to small farmers in the project area.  Currently, the situation is chan-
ging with the implementation of the Delta do Parnaiba project, which includes



~ 73 -                              ANNEX 2
Page 7
an applied research component oriented towards small farmers. In addition,
there is increasing cooperation between applied research and extension staff.
19.       The state system for improved seed production was recently es-
tablished and equipped during 1980. This system includes:
(a)  a State Seed Commission under the responsibility of the Secretary
of Agriculture with the participation of CIDAPI, CEPA, EMBRAPA,
EMATER, SUDENE, the Federal Ministry of Agriculture, DNOCS,
and the banks;
(b)  a production station (220 ha) with irrigation equipment for pro-
ducing basic seeds;
(c)  a seed laboratory in Teresina; and
(d)  five seed-processing units, including three in the project area.
This organization will be operational in 1981.
D. Issues and Main Constraints to Agricultural Development
Land Tenure and Use
20.       The large majority of farmers (about 78%) are landless.  They
have no land security.
21.       Large properties are usually characterized by a severe underuti-
lization of cultivable land with large sections remaining uncleared. These
properties are owned by traditional families generally lacking in the
agric:ultural entrepreneurship necessary to turn the farms into productive and
profitable businesses.
Technical Issues
22.       Labor productivity is on average very low, without substantial
differences between landless farmers and owner-operators (about 1 to 2 ha per
active worker). This is due to the traditional shifting cultivation system
combined with manual cropping. Newly cleared land is generally not destumped,
and thus any form of mechanization is unworkable. Under normal cultivation,
the two peak periods for labor, clearing and fencing (between 1 and 2 worker-
months per hectare) and harvesting (about 1 worker-month per hectare), limit
the size of the cropped area.
23.       Despite high rainfall, the risk of crop failure due to lack of
water is abnormally high. This situation results from a combination of
interrelated adverse factors, including:



- 74 -                              ANNEX 2
Page 8
(a) low infiltration, particularly at the opening of the
rainy season, coupled with increased run-off;
(b) low moisture-retention capacity of the soils;
(c) because of points (a) and (b), late planting time and a
long planting period; and
(d)  the wide use of varieties.which usually have a medium-to-
long vegetative cycle.
These factors discourage farmers from planting at the first good rains
because the encrusted soil does not allow for good moisture storage. Farmers
thus plant late (December/January) when rain frequency is higher, but lose the
benefits of early planting (soil nitrification, ashes, finer elements washed
away). In addition, because planting is slow when done manually, combined
with the low water-storage capacity of the soils, a farmer cannot plant his
area at once, but has to wait for successive rains. As a result, the risk of
crop failure increases with the lateness of planting, since crucial vegetative
phases, particularly maturation, take place when rain probability sharply
decreases at the end of the rainy season. This risk is even higher with the
long-cycle varieties currently in use.
24.       Erosion is much more damaging than commonly recognized in the
project area. There is no apparent gully erosion, except on the slopes,
but because of heavy rains combined with low surface soil permeability,
run-off is very high and all of the fine elements of soils are washed away.
As a result, fertility decreases dramatically when soil is not protected
either by crops or by mulching.
25.       Small owner-operators cannot follow the long bush fallow system
and soil fertility decreases under traditional technology. It is estima-
ted that under the usual shifting cultivation system, soils need about eight
years of bush fallow to recover natural fertility. A small-scale owner-
operator with less than 10 ha cannot afford to follow this fallow system, and
soil fertility is progressively lost under the traditional technology. This
situation is aggravated by the continued growth of the rural population.
26.        Crop productivity is usually low with large inter-yearly fluc-
tuations. This is mainly due to low soil fertility and a low level of crop
technology, which does not include soil management adapted to existing conditions,
improved seeds, organic or chemical fertilization, or pesticides.
27.       Implements for draft animals are not well adapted or diversified
to make the best use of animal traction. Unsuitable implements, coming
from Sao Paulo, are sold in Piaui; they are heavy and usually inadequate
for the specific conditions found in Piaui.



ANNEX 2
- 75 -                               Page 9
28.       Agricultural employment is mostly concentrated during the six to
seven-month rainy season since irrigation has not been significantly deve-
loped. Other non-farm job opportunities are limited.
29.       Crop opportunities are not diversified.  Traditional subsistence
production (rice, manioc, beans, corn) predominates. Gardening
and fruit cultivation are not widely practiced on small farms.
Institutional Issues
30.       Both agricultural extension and applied research have not suf-
ficiently focused their activities on small farmers to develop and diffuse
efficient technologies adapted to these farmers' conditions. Agricultural
research has been conducted thus far in experimental stations without suf-
ficiently addressing practical problems in the field, and agricultural ex-
tension has concentrated the majority of its activities on medium to large
farmers. In addition, extensionists have not been well trained in practic-
al applications.
II. AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT STRATEGY AND OBJECTIVES
A. Long-Term Development Objectives and Strategy
31.       The Parnaiba valley, which comprises most of the project area, is
probably one of the least agriculturally developed valleys in the Northeast,
especially in relation to its potential. Only about 10% of the area suitable
for agriculture is cultivated, although about 1.5 million hectares of forest
and bush fallow could be developed and irrigation potential is estimated in
the tens of thousands of hectares. One of the principal reasons for the
area's slow development is that the large majority of farmers (about 80% or
about 45,000 families) are virtually barred from taking advantage of the
area's potential. They do not own land; rather, they rent, under unfavorable
contracts, limited areas (about 3 ha per family) for subsistence farming under
a shifting cultivation system.  Most land is held by a few families, who do
not espouse principles of modern entrepreneurship.l/ At the same time, urban
centers (mostly Teresina and Parnaiba) are growing rapidly due to increasing
rural-to-urban migration, despite high levels of urban unemployment. Much of
the food supply for these cities comes from outside the state of Piaui. These
trends may be aggravated in the future by the increasing presence of specialized,
capital-intensive agrobusinesses (sugarcane, manioc, cattle-raising), encouraged
by both the very low price of land in Piaui and available federal subsidies
from such programs as Proalcool.
32.       Within a 20-year time frame, a significant factor to offset these
trends of rural migration and large-scale cash-crop farming would be the
emergence of a growing population of small to medium agricultural entrepreneurs
specialized in labor-intensive food production with modern technology, including
1/ See Annex 1 for a complete discussion of land tenure issues.



ANNEX 2
-76 -                               Page 10
irrigation, where possible.  These farmers would supply food to the growing
urban population, provide increased rural employment and income opportunities,
and consequently, help diminish rural-urban migration. An equilibrium could
be established between these small rural enterprises and the specialized
agrobusinesses mentioned above. To reach this equilibrium, strong support for
the presently poor, small agricultural producers in the project area would be
necessary. This support implies, in the first instance, access to land at
reasonable prices, as well as the provision of new technologies, including
natural resource conservation, technical assistance, marketing, and other
supporting services.
B. Project Objectives
33.       As the first step of a long-term rural development strategy,
the project would aim to:
(a) increase agricultural income and employment levels of
about 18% of the total population of small-scale
producers by developing rice as a cash crop;
(b) increase productive potential through the introduction of
adequate rotation, intercropping systems, water and soil
conservation practices to inhibit soil depletion through
erosion and, when possible, to raise fertility levels; and
(c) improve rural family access to food by increasing subsistence
production (manioc, beans, home garden vegetables) and by
developing small-livestock husbandry and small-scale cattle-
raising.
34.       In order to accomplish these goals, the project would attempt to:
(a) develop a single, low-cost, participatory, technically sound
approach to agricultural development based on an initial land
redistribution program; and
(b) establish and/or increase the institutional capacity at the state
and local levels to support small farmers in the proposed agri-
cultural development program; special attention would be paid to
the linkage between agricultural extension, applied re-
search, and the activities of the new state land institute to be
established under the project.



- 77 -
ANNEX 2
Page 11
C. Project Strategy
35.       Over the five-year investment period, the agricultural develop-
ment strategy would center around the following aspects:
(a) improvement of subsistence farming practices by extending
technologies already applied by a small fraction of farmers,
including several interdependent components, such as:
(i) reduction and eventual disappearance of shifting cultivation;
(ii) expansion of cultivated area by increased productivity of
family labor through mechanization with animal traction;
(iii) adoption of mixed farming systems; and (iv) widespread
diffusion of selected seeds, timely agricultural practices and
improved weeding to increase crop productivity;
(b) improvement of existing technologies and the generation of
some crop diversification; new crop alternatives are expected
to be developed by the applied research component;
(c) reduction of risk of crop failure due to lack of water by
promoting appropriate soil and crop management, including such
aspects as: (i) increasing soil permeability before the rainy
season by minimum tillage techniques and stubble mulch;
(ii) ensuring efficient soil coverage through mulching and
appropriate intercropping systems compatible with draft-animal
implements to decrease soil encrusting; (iii) increasing
water-storage capacity of soils by returning crop residues and
using manure; (iv) recommending early planting with draft-animal
sowing-machines; and (v) diffusing improved short-cycle varieties;
(d) initiation of irrigation on a relatively small-scale basis to
prepare for future development of the project area's water
resources;
(e) initiation of a soil conservation program for beneficiaries of
the land component, to serve later as a demonstration program
for diffusion to non-land beneficiaries; and
(f) reinforcement and upgrading of the technical training of
extension staff with a special emphasis on practical matters;
such training would be further extended to farmers and,
particularly, to new land beneficiaries.
An important part of this strategy would be the initial focus of technical
assistance and supporting services on those areas where redistribution is
taking place. In this way the link between the land redistribution process
and the agricultural strategy outlined above would be reinforced.



- 78-                              ANNEX 2
Page 12
III. TARGET AND ADOPTER GROUPS, CORRESPONDING FARM MODELS
A. Target Group
36.       Interventions for agricultural development under the project would
be adapted for use by and directed towards a target group of about 54,000
small producers, including:
(a) about 8,800 owner-operators of farms between 0 and 100 ha; they
represent the majority of landowners (70%), but only control 18%
of the land in the project area; and
(b) about 45,200 landless producers who rent land, mainly contracted
from large owners outside the target group.
B. Adopter Group
37.       It is anticipated that about 9,500 producers (about 18% of the
target group) would be assisted by supporting services (credit, extension,
and marketing), and thus would constitute the direct beneficiary or adopter
group. This adopter group would progressively apply the proposed technologies
and would be made up of:
(a) some 5,000 farmers (essentially landless) who would benefit
from the land redistribution program and be closely assisted in
their agricultural development;
(b) some 3,000 small owner-operators (34% of target-group owners)
who would benefit from all support services; and
(c) some 1,500 landless producers living in the vicinity of
purchased properties, who, through improvement of their renting
contracts, would be in a position to apply proposed technologies.
Among the beneficiaries of the land component, it is expected that some 1,500
farmers would have an average of 1 ha of irrigated land. The number of
landless beneficiaries (6,500 including some 5,000 land beneficiaries and
1,500 others) is modest when compared to the total landless target group
(about 14%). However, project activities would have a far greater impact than
the numbers imply by allowing for the acquisition of experience and the
building of basic institutional capacity to promote agricultural development
associated with land redistribution.



- 79 -                             ANNEX 2
Page 13
38.       The project would also indirectly benefit:
(a) some farm workers through increased employment opportu-
nities;
(b) a large percentage of "non-adopter" producers of the target
group through some assistance from supporting services
(marketing, seed production and input supply);
(c) a large number of non-target group farmers who would bene-
fit from some of the results of agricultural research; and
(d) individuals living in the vicinity of roads or water-supply
systems built under the project, though not benefiting from
any other component.
Farm Models
39.       Farm models have been conceived on the basis of a survey carried
out with 179 small-scale producers, including 101 owner-operators and 78
landless producers within the target group. The survey shows that average
area cultivated is significantly larger for the former group (5.3 ha) compared
with that cultivated by tenant farmers (3.9 ha). There are limited variations
in yields between the two categories, however, since the large majority of
farmers currently employ the same traditional technology and cropping patterns.
In addition, fluctuations between the three project subareas are too limited
to justify the conception of specific subarea farm models. Based on this
evidence, two different situations can be defined:
(a) small-scale owner-operators with farms below 100 ha and a
cropped area of about 5.3 ha with a labor force of 3.7 active
workers; and
(b) landless producers with 3 ha of cropped area and a labor force
of 2.4.
40.       From this point of departure, three basic farm models have been
designed:  one for small-scale owners, the second for the new land bene-
ficiaries, and the third for the landless. For each of these three models,
variations have been introduced in accordance with two levels of mechanization
(manual cropping and animal traction), the existence or the absence of irrigated
land, and two levels of crop technology (Annex 4).



- 80 -                             ANNEX 2
Page 14
IV. TECHNOLOGY OF PRODUCTION
A. Stabilized Farming in Association with Soil Conservation
41.       Agricultural development under the project would be based on the
progressive elimination of shifting cultivation and its replacement by a
more productive, stabilized system in close association with water and soil
conservation practices. Such a system would be established through:
(a) progressive land clearing and selective destumping to create
permanent plots allowing for more efficient cropping with
mechanization; land clearing and destumping would continue to
be carried out manually, but with improved tools (e.g. hand
winch) and tree-poisoning techniques. Land clearing would be
limited to cultivable areas according to soil quality and land
declivity; 1/ forest and natural pastures would be maintained
and/or upgraded in areas not suitable for agriculture. 2/ It
is anticipated that land clearing, in the case of relatively
dense forest, would reach 1 to 2 ha per farm family per year
and would be spread out over about five years. Higher rates
of clearing are expected in the case of bush fallow and
lighter vegetation;
(b) permanent fencing of permanent plots with barbed wire
instead of temporary heavy wooden fences;
(c) contour farming with strip cropping. In the particular case of
land beneficiaries, plot layout would be established in
accordance with topography;
(d) soil management to limit erosion and to simul-
taneously improve structural stability; soil preparation
would be generally limited to minimum tillage techniques
to increase infiltration; and
(e) rotation of adapted intercropping systems with permanent
soil coverage. Traditional intercropping systems would be
maintained, but improved to allow for mechanization. The
cropping period would be spread over four years, the first
three with rice intercropped with beans and maize, and the
fourth with manioc. Then cropping would be replaced by
improved pasture for the following four years. Modifications
would be introduced progressively in practiced intercroppings
according to the results of the applied research component,
especially with respect to identification of new crops.
1/   Soil reconnaissance surveys would be carried out as a basis for land
clearing.
2/   Prohibition of use of these areas for agriculture would be stipulated in the
contracts signed by land beneficiaries.



- 81 -                               ANNEX 2
Page 15
B.   Improved Farm Implements and Small Mechanization
42.       Improved farm implements and small mechanization would be the key
elements of the project for increasing farm family incomes through expected
dramatic increases in labor productivity. Such a mechanization program
would be in line with the new orientation of the Federal Government of Brazil
which gives much greater support than in the past to small mechanization
with draft animals, largely because of increasing fuel costs and its perceived
suitability to small-scale farming. Accordingly, new applied research
programs on small mechanization have been promoted in the Northeast, particu-
larly at the EMBRAPA regional experimental station at Petrolina, Pernambuco,l/
with international support from ICRISAT and bilateral French aid. New farm
implements from West Africa, India and Pakistan have been introduced, combined
with local implements, and modified when necessary. As a result, a new
generation of better-adapted farm equipment is now being manufactured and
recommended for use in the different states of the Northeast.
43.       The new farm implements would complement the equipment already used
by a few farmers in the area. The main features of the project's mechanization
program would be:
(a) its adaptation to soil conditions specific to the
project area and to soil and crop management practices
recommended under the project;
(b) its flexibility, considering the expected gradual shift by
project beneficiaries from improved manual cropping to relatively
more sophisticated and more comprehensive farm mechanization; a
variety of farm implements would be proposed to farmers to fit
in with their specific farm conditions and respective levels of
technology; and
(c) the complementarity between the different proposed solutions;
manual cropping could be progressively complemented by small
mechanization with draft animals, which, in turn, could be
associated with tractor-renting in the case of heavy work (land
clearing, preparation for clay soils, restitution of crop
residues, harvesting).
44.       Farm implements proposed under the project would fall under
four categories, three corresponding to different sources of power,
and the last, to post-harvesting equipment:
1/   Centro de Pesquisa Agricola para o Tropico Semi-Arido (CPATSA), located
in the Sao Francisco Valley, Pernambuco, a neighboring state of Piaui.



- 82 -                     ANNEX 2
Page 16
(a) Manual Cropping. Proposed tools would include manual winches
for land clearing, manual sowing machines (matraca) for rice
and other crops and scythes to replace the traditional knife
for rice harvesting. As a result, expected increases in labor
productivity would range from 30% to 80%, with total cropped
area increasing from 1.4 ha per worker to 2 ha per worker.
(b) Mechanization with One Draft Animal (donkey, mule, ox).
Proposed equipment would include a small cultivator/grader
with adjustable tools (particularly chisels) for grading and
for weeding, and a sowing machine. Such light equipment would
be well adapted to cases where destumping is not completed.
The expected increase in labor productivity would range from
100% to 300%, with total cropped area reaching up to 3.5 ha per
active worker.
(c) Mechanization with One Pair of Oxen. Proposed equipment would
be a mobile frame with a tool bar to which different types
of tooling for soil preparation, sowing, weeding, and transport
could be added.l/ With this equipment, average labor productivity
would increase fourfold, i.e., 5 ha of cropped area per worker.
With a complement of tractor-renting for heavy works, labor
productivity could reach more than 6 ha per worker.
(d) Post-Harvesting Equipment. This would include manual threshers
and winnowers for maize and rice.
45.       It is expected that the acquisition of farm implements would be
progressive with a demonstration phase during initial implementation. At full
development, it is anticipated that about 70% of project beneficiaries would
use small mechanization with animal traction while 30% would use improved
manual cropping.
C. Progressive Improvements in Crop Productivity
46.       Progressive improvements in crop productivity would complement
expected gains in labor productivity. These improvements are expected to
increase farm-family incomes, and particularly, to reduce the risk of crop
failure and corresponding wide annual fluctuations of income currently
affecting farmers. The proposed crop technologies do not involve major
changes from those currently used by more advanced farmers. They are mainly
based on highly productive varieties, with different lengths of growing cycle
(100 to 130 days), selected to adjust for varying rainfall and to spread
harvesting over a longer period. Improvements would also include timely
1/   Tropicultor and Ariana, tested in Pakistan and West Africa.



-83 -                           ANNEX 2
Page 17
cultural practices, particularly early sowing, frequent weedings, and effective
plant control. Fertilizer recommendations would be prudent and proposed
only when the above techniques were well applied. Fertilizer formulae have
been defined on the basis of the best available data on soil fertility levels
and on "technical packages" defined jointly by EMATER and EMBRAPA, in con-
junction with some of the more advanced producers. During project implemen-
tation, recommendations would be improved and adapted on the basis of further
soil-fertility analyses and the results of adaptive research. Yield assumptions
are relatively conservative since they are currently realized and some-
times substantially exceeded by advanced farmers. These assumptions should
be interpreted as averages for various technical levels. Individual producers'
yields would be determined by expertise and actual investment possibilities.
It is expected that yields would increase steadily during the first ten years
before reaching a stable level in Year 11 (eleven) due to the land clearing
program which would be spread over the first five to six years. Research
activities supported under the project are expected to generate higher yields
both before and after Year 10, but no attempt has been made to quantify these
possible benefits because of the substantial uncertainty affecting both their
timing and level.
47.       Rice would be the main cash crop under the project; a substantial
increase is expected in rice productivity over the first ten years of the
project and would constitute one of the project's major outputs. Under
present conditions, yields are very low and highly variable (0.70 t/ha with
sparse intercropping of corn, beans and manioc). With selected, chemically-
treated seeds, early planting, and better technology, it is anticipated that
the average yield would reach about 1.5 t/ha with limited annual fluctuations
at full development.l/ Rice would continue to be cultivated in rainfed
conditions, although some plots, located in the valley bottom, would be
cultivated under semi-irrigated or irrigated conditions. Rice would continue
to be intercropped with corn, beans and maize, although the proportion of
secondary crops sown per hectare of rice would be reduced as cropped area is
increased, as long as subsistence needs for the farm family would be met.
Yearly input coefficients and yield assumptions are given in Table 7.
48.       Gardening and small-livestock husbandry for home consumption would
be promoted by EMATER under the project, and nutrition training would be promoted
by social extensionists. A nutrition program is already being successfully
implemented through current extension efforts and would be reinforced under the
project. Main crops would be tomatoes, onions, green peppers, sweet potatoes,
1/   1.5 t/ha of rice would be reached by farmers using animal traction with
adequate technology and a light intercropping of corn and beans. The
small additional production of corn and beans has not been considered in
the financial and economic analysis.



-84 -                           ANNEX 2
Page 18
and some fruit trees, for which existing technical packages have shown good
results. Production from vegetable gardens, fruit trees, and small livestock
has not been taken into account in the financial and economic analysis, even
though a small part of this production is likely to be marketed, particularly
by farmers in the vicinity of urban centers.
D. Mixed Farming
49.       The project would aim at developing small-scale cattle raising in
association with agricultural activities, for the following reasons:
(a) improved pastures would replace bush fallow in the proposed
rotation and provide forage resources;
(b) development of self-sustained animal traction would require the
development and maintenance of a small herd at the farm-level;
(c) cattle raising, widely considered a status activity by small
farmers, would substantially complement their income through
cattle sales and provide them with milk for home consumption;
and
(d) manure production would raise the level of organic matter in
the soils and improve soil structure.
50.       The proposed technology does not involve major changes from that
currently used by advanced small cattle-raisers. This technology would be
based on improving animal feed through temporary pastures with mineral
supplements, and improving animal health and cattle management through
systematic vaccinations and associated prophylactic means. Under present
conditions, average cattle-raising performances are very low (reproduction
rate of about 25%, 0.15 animal per hectare of bush fallow, daily average
weight increase of about 0.12 kg/animal). With improved technology it is
expected that the herd growth rate would increase up to 50%-60%, with 0.5 to
1.0 animals per hectare of improved pasture and a daily average weight
increase of 0.3 kg/animal.
E.   Agricultural Development for Land Beneficiaries
51.        The land redistribution process would be closely associated with
a detailed agricultural development program specific to the local conditions
of each estate being redistributed. Such a program would be implemented
through strong support from agricultural extension and other delivery services.



ANNEX 2
- 85 -                           Page 19
Each land beneficiary would receive a lot, sized in accordance with INCRA's
recommendations, and specifications for the development of the lot during
the first years.l/ Adoption of minimum levels of proposed technology would be
encouraged. The set of specifications would follow the general strategy of
agricultural development proposed under the project, but would be specific to
local conditions. Such specifications would be prepared during the allotment
process (Annex 1).
V. IRRIGATION
52.       Within the proposed long-term development strategy, irrigation is
likely to be one of the major investments in the project area because of
abundant surface and underground water resources. Under the project, the
irrigation component would represent an initial phase to develop low-cost
approaches for small-scale irrigation and to strengthen state-level insitutional
capacity in order to prepare for further water development expected in a
follow-up investment period. Irrigation sub-projects would be chosen according
to a set of technical, economic, and social criteria (para 55). Under the
project, it is expected that 1,500 ha of small-scale irrigation would be
developed in the properties to be acquired and redistributed for about 1,500
land beneficiaries (i.e. 30% of the total number of land beneficiaries).
Project targets are modestly sized in relation to the project area's irrigation
potential and expected developments in the land acquisition and redistribuiton
program. Nonetheless, these targets are relatively ambitious since small-scale
irrigation is a recent activity within the state and current insitutional
capacity is limited. During each annual planning exercise, both annual and
cumulative targets would be reviewed and appropriately adjusted in accordance
with past experience and the level of institutional development.
53.       It is expected that on average each beneficiary would receive a 20
to 30 ha lot with a 1 ha irrigated plot, although certain situations may not
conform to this model. The 1,500 ha of irrigated land would be planted mainly
in rice with progressive attainment of double-cropping at full development.
High-value crops, such as fruits and vegetables, could also be developed under
irrigation in a long-term perspective, but outside of home-gardening, they
would not be promoted during the project execution period because of the
technology required and limited local marketing outlets.
54.       Small-scale irrigation schemes under the project are likely to be
concentrated in the Delta do Parnaiba Sub-area, which presents the state's
highest irrigation potential, particularly in the varzeas, low-lying flood
plains (Annex 2, para 5). These schemes, for both irrigation and drainage,
would be developed with a low-cost technology consisting of protective dikes
1/ 35 to 40 ha in the project area; such an area is defined by its potential
capacity for annual income generation which corresponds to three minimum
salaries (about US$1,900).



- 86 -                          ANNEX 2
Page 20
and small pumping stations on the riverside. In a limited number of other
cases, irrigation would also be developed upland, by digging tube-wells,
and pumping underground water when easily and cheaply available. With
regard to land already acquired, a 10,000 ha estate along the Parnaiba river
presents adequate conditions for varzea-type irrigation development, and a
16,500 ha estate is presently being surveyed for the possible development of
irrigation by pumping underground water. In both cases, irrigation potential
is presently being assessed, and proposals for irrigation development would be
received during the project-s first year planning exercise.
A. Criteria for Small-Scale Irrigation Schemes
55.       The final decision to build a small-scale irrigation project on
an acquired property to be redistributed would depend on a set of physical,
technical, economic, and social criteria. Each case would be carefully
reviewed in light of existing surveys, additional data to be gathered during
the land acquisition and allotment phase (Annex 1, para 25), and supplementary
studies specific to irrigation. These criteria include:
(a)  Soil Quality.  In addition to general soil surveys, which
already exist or would be carried out for land allotment,
detailed topographic and soil surveys would be conducted
in those areas with good irrigation potential, which would
be initially defined on the basis of photo interpretation.
Particular attention would be paid to the clay content and
moisture retention capacity of the soils in relation to rice
cultivation.
(b)  Hydrological Aspects.  Hydrological aspects of the lower
Parnaiba have already been extensively studied by SUDENE
and DNOCS, with the help of consultants. Nevertheless,
complementary studies would be conducted when needed,
particularly in the gauging of secondary tributaries and
streams affecting selected varzeas.
(c)  Water Requirements.  The water requirements would be calculated
according to the Modified Penman Method following FAO guidelines.
For rice, land preparation requirements would include water
for saturation, evaporation, and percolation. During the
cultivation period, water requirements for percolation losses
would be provided. Overall irrigation efficiency is expected
to be about 50%.
(d)  Economic Criteria.  Each sub-project would be subjected to an
economic rate of return analysis, and no investment would be
made unless the ERR was at least 15%. This 15% floor has been
established considering rice cultivation with doubling cropping
at full development and yields between 2.5 and 3.5 t/ha per
cropping. lIt is expected that most schemes would not exceed
US$5,000 (September 19Vo prices) per 'nectare.  )gsicCa ccatiz-
gencies of 20% would be added to estimated construction costs.



ANNEX 2
- 87 -                          Page 21
(e) Population Density. In order to minimize population relocation
and once other criteria have been met, preference would be
given to irrigation sub-projects where the number of potential
beneficiaries for land redistribution is too high to provide
each family with an "INCRA module" under rainfed farming
conditions. Socio-economic surveys to be carried out for
land acquisition and distribution would provide the basis
for this criteria.
(f)  Beneficiary Participation.  INTERPI, with the assistance
of EMATER, would promote the creation of associations for
future water users. Prior to construction, a written agreement
(convenio) would be established, specifying management arrange-
ments and charges. These associations would be responsible
for collecting water charges and would also constitute an
appropriate channel for technical assistance. In most cases,
it is expected that these associations would carry out operation
and maintenance activities themselves. In cases where this
is not feasible, part of the water charges would be used
to contract private maintenance firms. EMATER-PI would keep
a list of such firms and provide technical assistance to
the associations in choosing appropriate contractors.
B. Organization and Management
56.       The irrigation component would be coordinated by INTERPI's technical
services department, Initial selection of irrigation sites would be carried
out by this department; subsequent feasibility and project studies would
be conducted by consultants selected by INTERPI on the national market.
Initial feasibility studies would be presented to the Bank for approval prior
to the completion of detailed designs and the commencement of construction.
Simultaneously, INTERPI would establish a State Water Commission (SWC) which
would eventually be responsible for reviewing these preliminary studies
and deciding on their adequacy before construction. Such a commission would
be made up of representatives from the State Secretariat of Agriculture,
EMATER, DNOCS, and chaired by the Director of Technical Services of INTERPI.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the commission would be
set up no later than October 1, 1981, and that, unless the Bank otherwise
agrees, feasibility studies for small-scale irrigation schemes would be sent
to the Bank for approval prior to the completion of detailed designs and
implementation of the schemes. Construction of irrigation schemes would be
awarded to private constructors selected on the national market under local
standard competitive bidding procedures, which are acceptable to the Bank.
Operation and maintenance would be the responsibility of a water users
association (para 54). Assurances were obtained during negotiations that
these associations would be set up prior to construction. Both the associations
and individual farmers involved would receive technical assistance on proper
water use and management from existing specialized staff within EMATER-PI.



ANNEX 2
- 88 -                          Page 22
C. Irrigation and Water Development Charges
57.       Irrigation and water development charges have been defined according
to federal guidelines prepared by the Ministry of Interior. Under these
guidelines, water charges are the sum of two fees: the first aims to recover
investment capital in relation to farmers' repayment capacity; and the second
is based on operation and maintenance costs and the quantity of water used.
In the case of Piaui, it is expected that investment capital would average
US$3,000/ha and 0 and M about US$128/ha. Annual charges of US$86/ha for
capital recovery and US$128/ha for 0 and M would permit full recovery of the
irrigation component costs over a period of about 35 years at 0% interest with
a three-year grace period in the case of investment recovery. By discounting
both water charges and the development costs plus 0 and M cost over a 35-year
project life at a 12% rate, the ratio of present worth of the charges over the
present worth of costs indicates a cost recovery of about 37%. Assuming an
increase in investment cost up to US$5,000 without changing the above charges,
cost recovery would be about 27%. The rent recovery ratio has been calculated
on the basis of the three farm models with irrigation; it would range between
30% and 40% according to the various expected levels of technology in rice
cultivation. Annual development charges then would average US$86/ha for
recovery and US$128/ha for 0 and M, i.e, US$214/ha/year for irrigated land.
These charges would represent an average of 17% to 20% of the gross value
of rice production on irrigated land assuming a double-cropping and a yield
of 3 t/ha/cycle. These charges are acceptable considering the current and
expected levels of income of the potential beneficiaries, and are in line
with standard practices in irrigation projects in the Northeast (Annex 4).
Charges each year would be established according to the area, farmer's actual
repayment capacity, and the rate of inflation. Before the completion of the
works for each irrigation scheme, the State Water Commission would establish a
set of water charges which would be sent to the Bank for its review. During
negotiations, assurances to this effect were obtained. Charges related to cost
recovery of initial investment would be turned over by the associations to
INTERPI according to pre-established written agreement (para 55(f)).
VI. APPLIED RESEARCH PROGRAM
58.       The proposed applied research program would have six objectives:
(a) to refine, improve, and adapt to the target group existing
advanced technologies for the main existing crops and cattle-
raising, according to the relative importance of each in the
total output of the project. The crop program would particularly
focus on varieties, chemical and organic fertilization, and
plant protection, while the cattle-raising program would focus
on pasture development;
(b)  to monitor the small mechanization program, and improve
and adapt farm implements to local conditions;



ANNEX 2
- 89 -                          Page 23
(c) to test other crop alternatives (sorghum, groundnuts, annual
cotton, soybean) and new intercropping systems with animal
traction;
(d) to test vegetable cultivation (tomatoes, sweet potatoes,
onion, garlic) under irrigation and propose improved technical
packages for home gardening;
(e) to test different alternatives for soil management (minimum
tillage, harrowing, plowing, return of crop residues) and
propose adapted solutions; monitor the soil conservation
program, particularly in relation to land redistribution;
test weed control techniques; and,
(f) to set up a permanent mechanism for continuous transfer of
technology from applied research to extension, with farmers-
participation.
59.       This applied research program would be implemented at four levels:
(a) Experimental Station of Teresina.' This station is located
in the middle of the project area and is representative
of the ecological conditions most commonly found throughout.
Experiments conducted under strictly controlled conditions
would aim at sorting out new techniques for the project area
with a focus on new crops, new varieties, fertilization formulae
and plant protection;
(b) Complementary Sub-stations (Batalha and Sao Pedro de Piaui).
Experiments would permit a first adaptation to conditions
specific to the Delta do Parnaiba and Medio Parnaiba sub-areas.
(c) Experiments at the Farm Level. These experiments would be
conducted with the actual participation of farmers. They
would permit the testing of farmers reaction to, and the
appropriateness and benefits of, the new techniques coming
from the experimental station and sub-stations in order to
gauge any difficulties for possible further diffusion.
(d) Demonstration Fields. These fields would be cultivated by the
farmers themselves under EMATER supervision with the full
application of recommended technical packages. These fields,
although not experimental in the pure sense, would be the
ultimate checking points before mass diffusion.



-90                             ANNEX 2
Page 24
60.       The applied-research program would be coordinated and implemented
by EMBRAPA with the particular support of the regional EMBRAPA experi-
mental station of Petrolina in the area of small mechanization. The EMIBRAPA
research team would be made up of a coordinator and ten assistants, seven of
whom are highly trained agriculturists with research experience and extension
backgrounds. The research team would work very closely with EMATER and
farmers' representatives. Every year a research council, made up of farmers'
representatives, and representatives from EMATER, EMBRAPA, CEPA-PI and the
Project Unit, would meet together to review the results of the first research
campaign, to sort out techniques ready to be disseminated to farmers and to
decide on the research program for the following campaign. The total cost of
this experimentation program over the five-year project period (1981-1986) is
estimated at about Cr$ 134.3 million (US$2.4 million) as detailed in Annex 3.
VII.  FARM BUDGETS
61.       From the three basic farm models designed for owner-operators,
land beneficiaries, and landless farmers (para 39-40), ten cases have been
analyzed to test project recommendations. These cases were defined in relation
to levels of mechanization (manual cropping and animal traction), levels of
crop technology (intermediate and high) and the presence or absence of irrigated
land. The analyses were done under the assumption that the traditional
cropping system (rice, corn, beans, and manioc) would continue, but would
gradually improve through the introduction of a mixed farming system with
the complementary development of cattle raising. It is likely, however,
that some crop diversification would modify these traditional intercroppings
through the introduction of new crops as a result of the applied research
component. In each of the ten cases, the proposed land improvements, coupled
with simple technical packages to be extended under the project, were found
to be highly profitable for target farmers, even though the rate of adoption
and the time required to attain full development have been cautiously estimated
(limited expectation of crop increases during project's first years and about
10 years to reach full development). The most illustrative farm situations
among the ten are presented in Annex 4.
62.       Comparison of the with and without project situation for the
illustrative farm models shows the potential for income generation related
to project activities. For land beneficiaries, net farm family income would
rise from Cr$50,000 (US$900) to an average of Cr$210,000 (US$3,750) in rainfed
agriculture, and to an average of Cr$235,000 (US$4,200) with an additional
1 ha plot of irrigated land. For owner-operators, net farm family income
would rise from Cr$100,000 (US$1,800) to an average Cr$230,000 (US$4,100).
For the landless beneficiaries, the expected increase of family income is
substantially lower, from Cr$50,000 (US$900) to Cr$70,000 (US$1,250). This
limited increase is explained by their limited investment capacity and their
continued dependence on landlords; progress would result from the introduction
of some low-cost improved technology (improved seeds, early planting, and
better marketing).  Average results for the ten cases are presented i-n
Table 8.3 in the main text.



ANNEX 2
-91-                            Page 25
63.       Employment.  As a result of the developments proposed under the
project, primary agricultural employment is expected to increase substantially.
At full development, annual labor requirements on target farms would reach
about 56,000 man-years, an increase of about 20,650 man-years, or about
58% over present labor use. The extent to which these requirements could
be met by family labor would vary significantly according to season and
farm size. On average, some 70% would come from family labor, with the
remainder hired within the Vale do Parnaiba area. The main employment effect
of the project would be a fuller, more evenly distributed utilization of
current labor resources available in the project area.
VIII. PRODUCTION ESTIMATES
64.       Based on the farm survey carried out by the project preparation
team in the project area, the estimated pre-project value of production
for the target group in 1980 was about US$12.8 million, of which 41% came
from rice, 40% from manioc, 11% from corn, 5% from beans, and 3% from cattle-
raising. It is likely, in line with recent experience, that without the
project the total production of the target group would remain at roughly
the current level, since the implementation of possible improvements in
crop technologies would be severely limited by the present land tenure
situation and partially nullified by the current process of soil erosion and
depletion.
65.       Incremental production under the project has been estimated based
on the evolution of the ten farm models. Excluding small-animal husbandry
and vegetable production, the incremental annual output would be equivalent
to US$32 million, bringing the total value of production to about US$44.8
million annually at full development. This increase would result mainly
from an increase in cultivated area (more than 100%) and to a lesser extent
from improvements in crop technologies (about 80% for rainfed rice).
66.       Most of the value of incremental output is expected to come from
rice (49%), manioc (28%), and beef (15%), with the remaining 8% coming from
corn and beans. A summary of production targets and the incremental value
of production is presented in Table 7.1 of the main text.
