- 127 -                            ANNEX 10
Page 1
NORTHEAST BRAZIL
PIAUI RURAL DEVELPOMENT PROJECT
MONITORING AND EVALUATION
A. Monitoring
1.        Project activities would be monitored through a three-pronged
system: (i) regular sub-coordinator action; (ii) reporting by implementing
agencies, and (iii) information obtained from project beneficiaries. Each of
these sub-systems would complement each other, obtaining information through
different approaches and scopes. They would give the Project Coordinator a
satisfactory view of project implementation, alerting him both to problems
which might require immediate action (by the executing agencies, by himself,
or by the Secretaries), as well as those which might develop over time.
2.        The monitoring system was devised taking into consideration several
constraints which, by their very nature, are somewhat divergent or even
conflicting: (i) the monitoring system should be sufficiently large and
comprehensive to give project management enough information to adequately
oversee project implementation; (ii) it should be simple enough to be easy
and relatively inexpensive to administer, to deliver results with minimal
delays, and to prevent flooding management with a volume of information
too large to be easily understood; (iii) it should take into full account
and incorporate as far as possible existing information flows, and
particularly, information already required by POLONORDESTE as part of its own
monitoring and disbursement system; and (iv) it should be flexible, properly
responding to changing management needs or field conditions.
Monitoring by Sub-Coordinators
3.        The five technical sub-coordinators and the three regional field
coordinators would continuously be in contact with both the executing agencies
and the beneficiaries. Institutional reports, interviews, participation
in meetings and personal observation in the field would allow them to obtain
a continuous flow of information. Although they basically would check the
same set of indicators on which the implementing agencies would periodically
report (para 4), the sub-coordinators' monitoring would have three advantages:
(i) it would be continuous; (ii) it would be able to review qualitative
variables which cannot be efficiently handled by the reporting system;
and (iii) it would have a minimal feedback time. Also, sub-coordinators would
be required to go to the field or to contact some of the executing agencies
whenever problems were detected in order to identify causes and suggest
appropriate actions. These special searches by the sub-coordinators would
parallel the special evaluation studies referred to below (para 21) and might
then be called "special monitoring studies".



- 128 -                            ANNEX 10
Page 2
Reporting by Implementing Agencies
4.        The executing agencies would periodically report on a set of key
indicators to the relevant coordinators. These indicators cover all relevant
fields and are expected to leave no major blind spots. If any blind spot is
discovered during implementation, the list would be reviewed and amended. The
key indicators cover most components with approximately the same density; a
more intensive coverage, however, is provided for those aspects considered
crucial for proper project implementation or where major project risks have
been identified (e.g. evolution of land markets and prices; technical change
and fishing effort in the fishing communities; technical support to
cooperatives).
5.        An effort was made to define the indicators so that indicators:
(i) measure a single variable; no composite indicators are included; (ii) are
as representative as possible, i.e. changes or trends in these variables
should reflect what is actually happening over as wide a range of project
activities as possible; and (iii) are as predictive as possible, i.e. changes
in them would foretell forthcoming problems as far in advance as possible.
Most of the indicators selected are already a part of the regular information
flows of the agencies concerned; only a minimum of new or additional variables
were included. The set of indicators to be monitored under the project is
fully compatible with the set of variables regularly monitored by POLONORDESTE.
6.        The set of variables to be monitored, the description of each one,
the presentation format, and the periodicity of reporting were discussed with
most of the agencies involved. The agencies found the variables appropriate
and feasible to report on. Required changes were introduced into the original
proposals, and the agencies committed themselves to provide the necessary
information.
7.        Table 1 includes a list of the variables to be monitored and indicates:
(a)  whether this information is already required by the POLONORDESTE
system; if not, whether it is already collected for internal
institutional purposes or whether it is new or additional.
In some cases, it may seem that the list of new or additional
variables is too long, but such cases usually refer to new
activities to be initiated under the project;
(b)  the frequency with which this information should be provided
and the agency responsible for providing this information. An
effort was made to standardize the frequency of reporting; in
most cases, variables would be reported on a quarterly basis.
Other variables, generally those reflecting activities which
develop over a longer term (i.e. research) would be reported
annually or semi-annually. Some highly sensitive variables,
those essential to project implementation or for which immediate
corrective action is possible (e.g. input supply or marketing),
are to be reported on a monthly basis throughout the critical
period;



- 129 -                            ANNEX 10
Page 3
(c) the agency responsible for providing the information; and
(d) the information to be passed on to the Project Coordinator.
8.        Information would be sent by the agencies to the Planning, Monitoring,
and Evaluation Sub-Unit in the PMU. It would be checked, analyzed, consolidated,
and subsequently passed on to the technical sub-coordinators. The sub-coordinators
would be responsible for reacting to any danger signals requiring feedback
or corrective actions. Only those pieces of information which give an overview
of project activities, and those which reflect the most crucial project
activities, would be passed on to the Project Coordinator (as indicated in the
last column of Table 1). The anticipated monitoring information flow is shown
in Chart 1. A copy of the quarterly reports provided to the Project Coordinator
wou:Ld be sent to the Bank for information. This monitoring sub-system would
start functioning on April 1, 1981. The deadlines for the implementing agencies
to deliver information to the PMU would be the same as those established by
POLONORDESTE.
Beneficiary Sounding
9.        The institutional reporting sub-system would give the Project
Coordinator an overview of how project activities are being performed, but it
would not give him any feeling as to whether project activities are being
perceived or received by project beneficiaries, or whether beneficiaries are
reacting in the way the project anticipates. To compensate for this, a third
monitoring sub-system would be introduced. Information would be collected
monthly on ten or twelve key indicators, from a random sample of beneficiaries.
Sample size would be extremely small: five beneficiaries in each of the three
project areas per month. Results would not be statistically significant, but
the objective of the sub-system would be to detect whether the services
provided by the project agencies are being received by project beneficiaries
and whether they are adopting the main technological changes proposed by the
project. No attempt would be made to measure the magnitude of the change,
only to detect whether the change is occurring or not. Actual measurements of
the magnitude of the changes induced by the project would be attempted through
the mid-term and ex-post evaluation exercises (para 18).
10.       Questions to be asked of land beneficiaries would be kept to a bare
minimum to ensure practicality. Longer questionnaires would require more
interviewing time and place a heavier burden on both respondents and interviewers.
More questions would require a longer data processing and analysis time,
detracting from the intended simplicity.  The questions would be extremely
simple, usually requiring a yes/no answer. Only in a few cases would figures
on area be required in order to determine whether a certain practice is being
adopted by farmers. These monthly interviews would be carried out by the
three regional field coordinators; the answers would be recorded on a pre-
addressed postcard.1/ This type of procedure would serve a double purpose. On
the one hand, it would make transfer of the answered forms to the PM&E Sub-Unit
1/ This system was successfully tested under the Philippines Third Education
Project, Loan 1224 T/PH.



ANNEX 10
- 130 -                           Page 4
much faster, because the cards would be delivered directly by mail, instead
of going all the way up and down the hierarchical structure. Delays and
data alteration would be minimized. On the other hand, it would help to keep
the number of questions short because of the sheer reduced space for answers
that such a card provides.
11.       The information system for land beneficiaries would be started
in the Delta do Parnaiba and Medio Parnaiba sub-areas on September 1, 1981.
In the Teresina sub-area, it would start when at least 120 beneficiaries
have been settled. The list of questions is presented in Table 2. The
monthly and annual tabulation sheets kept by the PM&E Sub-Unit would be the
only "output" of this monitoring sub-system. They would be passed on to the
technical and regional coordinators and the Project Coordinator, and copied to
the Bank for information. The list of questions for fishermen beneficiaries
is currently being prepared. Five fishermen would be interviewed each month.
It is currently anticipated that the questionnaire would be administered by
the fisheries sub-coordinator; therefore, no pre-addressed answering card is
required.
Overview
12.       In brief, this three-pronged monitoring system would produce all
the information project management is expected to require for daily decision-
making, as well as for the annual planning exercise. It would provide manage-
ment with news on important developments on very short notice through the
coordinators. It would permit handling both quantitative and qualitative
information, and it would obtain information from the farmers themselves on
how they are receiving and reacting to project activities. Throughout project
implementation, the monitoring system would remain flexible, open to modific-
ation whenever justified by changing management needs or field circumstances.
The Project Coordinator would discuss with the State Secretaries of Planning
and Agriculture the information that they would like to periodically receive
in order to remain abreast of project developments.
B. Evaluation
13.       Project effects and impact would be evaluated through three different
instruments: (i) two major evaluation exercises; (ii) several special evaluation
short studies; and (iii) a model-based annual desk evaluation for the fisheries
component. Each of these would assess project results from a different
perspective. The first implies a global approach to the whole spectrum of
project effects and impact. The second focuses on particular implementation
problems and--should they appear--puzzling or unanticipated effects. The
third, based on the fishermen income model developed at appraisal, would keep
track of changes in income levels and distribution utilizing data gathered by
the monitoring system.
14.       Evaluation activities try to reach a proper balance between costs
and benefits. The evaluation scheme is comprehensive, but commensurate with
the size of the project. Many other variables could have been selected for
analysis. Nevertheless, a conscious effort--fully shared by Bank and project
staff--was made to keep evaluation aims at a more modest level. Current



- 131 -                            ANNEX 10
Page 5
design is comprehensive enough to cover developments concerning the most
important variables that project activities would be expected to affect:
land, technology, production, and income. Even in these central aspects, only
some---usually simple--variables were chosen, which would become gross indicators
of main developments under the project. For instance, only the most obvious
indicators of technological change would be assessed. No attempt would be
made to directly measure variables such as yields or income. Some indication
of the former would be obtained basically through farmer recollection (for
fishermen, information on fish landings and processing would be available on a
continuous basis), while the latter would be approached through the proxies of
volume of production and major expenditure indicators.
15.       Limited attempts would be made to assess other variables which,
although vitally important, are not the primary project goals, e.g. literacy,
and some indicators of living conditions such as housing, water supply, and
main household belongings. However, no attempt is envisaged to measure
standard of living on a broader scope, health conditions (except for some
rudimentary indications of morbidity and mortality)l/ or nutritional status.
16.       The level of statistical significance to be utilized for project
evaluation activities is rather at the lower end of commonly accepted significance
levels. This level is estimated to be sufficient for the major purposes
sought (improved annual programming; preparation of similar or follow-up
projects; accountability of funds spent; and assessment of project design) and
would result in considerable saving in sample size and thus, in data processing
and analysis.
17.       In keeping evaluation exercises relatively simple, the "costs"
considered were not only financial costs, but organizational, processing,
and staffing requirements as well. Even though the major evaluations would be
contracted out, larger, more comprehensive exercises would call for more
experts in the PM&E Sub-Unit to draft terms of reference and bidding invitations,
to prequalify bidders and evaluate proposals, and to supervise the entire
process. Interviews with beneficiaries and project staff would be longer,
implying a more tiresome process for both interviewers and interviewees and a
lower quality control, thus resulting in lower quality data. More interviews
and more questions would result in higher costs, especially for data coding,
punching, processing, and analyzing. Results would be obtained much later,
and perhaps even too late for use by project management.2/
1/ This "neglect" is not without a reason. No disease occurrence or spread
is anticipated as a consequence of project activities. Malaria has been
eradicated in the project area. Irrigation works would be quite minor and
would, if anything, improve sanitary conditions in the area.
2/ This is particularly true of the mid-term evaluation exercise, which is
the most crucial for direct project purposes since it would permit the
introduction of changes in project design, improvements in annual programming,
and modifications in the monitoring system. Early results are also a crucial
feature of the special implementation studies.



ANNEX 10
-132-                              Page 6
Major Evaluation Exercises
18.       Currently, potential project beneficiaries are at very low
technological levels. It is anticipated that it would take at least two
or three years of intensive extension and credit efforts for them to start
adopting some of the practices promoted by the project. Therefore, there
is no need to evaluate project effects and impact too early or too often.
Only two major evaluation exercises would be carried out: (i) a mid-term
evaluation, to be initiated during project year 3, which would cover develop-
ments up to project year 3 inclusive; and (ii) an ex-post evaluation after
project completion (anticipated for March 1986), which would be carried out
immediately thereafter (April-December 1986). These evaluations would involve
a thorough review of all information already collected through the monitoring
system, as well as detailed visits to the project agencies and interviews with
current and former executives and staff. Information on project effects and
impact on beneficiaries would be collected through statistically significant
surveys, covering some 50 fishermen and 210 land beneficiaries (about 30
interviews in each of the two main ecological zones--chapadas and lowlands--in
each of the three sub-areas, and 30 interviews in areas benefiting from
small-scale irrigation). A "control group" of some 30 fishermen and 100
farmers would also be interviewed. Both evaluations would aim at measuring
changes in the technology utilized by fishermen and farmers, as well as levels
of capitalization, production, income, and living standards.
19.       These major evaluations would require baseline data representing
the pre-project situation. For land beneficiaries, baseline data would be
collected as they are being registered as potential candidates for land
allocation under the project. For this purpose, a special form has been
prepared which would be attached to the registration cards already in use.
The form contains questions about current production, technology, marketing,
assets, income, and standard of living. This method of data collection
through registration (an activity which would extend over several years), is
made possible by the fact that project activities would be fairly concentrated
in space; therefore, they would most certainly produce no immediate noticeable
effects on farmers who were not direct beneficiaries--at least in the short
run. The case of fishermen, however, is different. They live concentrated in
small villages. Therefore, project activities would, to some extent, affect
most of them; baseline data on the same variables listed above would have to
be collected before project activities start in a significant way. Accordingly,
a baseline survey would be conducted by extension agents between April and
August 1981 in each of the ten fishing communities. Both for farmers and
fishermen, baseline data would be collected for some 10% of the beneficiary
population (about 500 farmers; about 200 fishermen).
20.       Both evaluations would be contracted out to entities external
to the project (consulting firms, universities). Terms of reference and
bidding invitations would be prepared by the PM&E Sub-Unit in the PMU and sent
to the Bank for approval. Contracts would require the delivery of two or
tWtzee i      tet-im  'epwts (e.g. on t'ne po'cpose& sample and    so6naiTe; &xitg
the sampling; and after a preliminary analysis of the information collected
from all sources). These would allow monitoring of the evaluation exercise by
the PM&E Sub-Unit, to prevent surprises at a later stage on the aims, approach,
design, scope, or methods of the evaluation.



-  133                            ANNEX 10
Page 7
Special Evaluation Studies
21.       The project would provide funds each year for one or two special
evaluation studies. These would be short studies on specific problems
affecting project implementation. The need for such evaluations would be
identified by the Project Coordinator and technical sub-coordinators. The
former would decide on the execution of studies involving up to six staff-
months of PMU personnel. Studies involving more than six staff-months would
require the previous approval of the Bank. The final report would be sent to
the Bank.
22.       The first of this series of short studies has already been identified.
Land discrimination would be started under the project in year 2. In order
to prepare INTERPI for this task and obtain information on the main parameters
required for programming (e.g. number and type of staff required; area which
a discrimination team could cover per year; cost per hectare or per property;
percentage of the total area improperly occupied by private farms), the
project would recruit and finance consultants to review and assess the
experiences from two or three discrimination schemes carried out by INCRA
elsewhere in Brazil. This study would be conducted during the second half of
1981; results would be available early in project year 2.
23.       It is anticipated that most of the short studies would be conducted
by PMU staff (usually, staff of the PM&E Sub-Unit).  However, in some cases,
such as that mentioned in para 22, consultants would be utilized.
Annual Desk Evaluation of Fishermen Income Levels and Distribution
24.       An annual desk evaluation of changes in income and income distri-
bution would be carried out for the fisheries component. On the basis of the
data collected through the monitoring system (number of fishing days, number
of owners with different kinds of crafts and gear, volume of fish landed, and
prices), the income level/distribution model prepared at appraisal would be
updated each year, permitting an overview of sector development.

(b) (more than/similar to/less than last year)

(a) hand winch

(c) scythe
(d) cultivator
(e) other seeding machines
(f) multi-cultor/poly-cultor
(g) manual threshers and winnowers

(a) non-associated rice culture
(b) small irrigation
(c) improved rice seeds
(d) other improved seeds
(e) new cropping practices
(f) fertilizers
(g) mineral nutrients for livestock

(a) were you visited by a technical extension agent
(b) by a social extension agent
(c) did you participate in any demonstration visits
(d) did you participate in any courses
